Then reduce. Actually, let me try a different approach. Let me keep the simple kernel (one program per (batch, head)) but process the sequence in a way that maximizes L2 cache hits for GQA. If I schedule programs for the same (batch, kv_head) group close together, the L2 should cache the KV data. In my current grid layout: pid = b * H + h For b=0, h=0..3 (same kv_head 0): pids 0, 1, 2, 3 For b=0, h=4..7 (same kv_head 1): pids 4, 5, 6, 7 These are adjacent, so they should be scheduled close together. But the GPU schedules programs across SMs, and each SM has its own L2 partition (or shares L2 with nearby SMs). On modern NVIDIA GPUs, the L2 cache is shared across all SMs. So if program 0 loads KV for (b=0, hkv=0) and programs 1-3 run on different SMs, they should all find the KV data in L2. But the timing matters: if program 1 starts before program 0 finishes loading all the KV data, program 1 won't find the data in L2 and will load from DRAM again. For this to work, programs 0-3 need to be synchronized so that program 0 loads all KV first, then programs 1-3 access it from L2. But this is not how GPUs work - programs run independently. So the L2 caching approach is unreliable. The GQA sharing kernel (loading KV once and processing all query heads) is the way to go. But with GQA sharing, we have fewer programs. Split the sequence into tiles, one program per (batch, kv_head, seq_tile). After all tiles finish, a second kernel reduces the partial results: - For each (batch, query_head): combine num_seq_blocks partial softmax results - This is a simple reduction: combine (m_i, l_i, o_i) from each tile The reduction kernel is trivial: - For each (b, h), read num_seq_blocks (m, l, o) tuples - Combine them using the standard online softmax merge: m = max(m_1, m_2, ...) l = sum(l_j * exp(m_j - m)) o = sum(o_j * l_j * exp(m_j - m) / l) Wait, the merge is: - m_global = max(m_1, m_2, ..., m_K) - l_global = sum(l_j * exp(m_j - m_global)) - o_global = sum(o_j * (l_j * exp(m_j - m_global) / l_global)) Actually, let me think about this more carefully. Each tile produces: - m_tile: running max of scores in that tile - l_tile: running sum of exp(score - m_tile) for that tile - o_tile: running weighted sum / l_tile? Or unnormalized? I should store unnormalized results: - m_tile: max score in tile - l_tile: sum of exp(score - m_tile) for scores in tile - o_tile: sum of exp(score - m_tile) * V for scores in tile (unnormalized) Then merge: - m = max(m_1, ..., m_K) - l = sum(l_j * exp(m_j - m)) - o = sum(o_j * exp(m_j - m)) - Final output: o / l This is correct. The scratch buffer needs to store (m, l, o) for each (batch, query_head, seq_tile): - m: 1 float per (b, h, tile) = B * H * num_tiles floats - l: same - o: D floats per (b, h, tile) = B * H * D * num_tiles floats For shape 2: B=4, H=64, D=128, num_tiles=16 - m + l: 2 * 4 * 64 * 16 * 4 = 131 KB - o: 4 * 64 * 128 * 16 * 4 = 8388 KB = 8.2 MB 8.2 MB is a lot of scratch space. Before implementing a complex two-pass approach, let me try a simpler optimization: process multiple pages per iteration (BLOCK_SEQ > page_size) to amortize loop overhead. The issue with BLOCK_SEQ > page_size is that consecutive tokens may span multiple pages, requiring multiple page lookups. But with pre-loaded page numbers, this is just a gather. For BLOCK_SEQ = 64 (4 pages worth): - Create a (BLOCK_SEQ,) tensor of physical page offsets - Load K from multiple pages - This is a gather load, which is less efficient than contiguous loads Hmm, gather loads might hurt bandwidth. Let me think... Actually, within a block of BLOCK_SEQ = 64 consecutive tokens starting at a page boundary, all 64 tokens are in the same 4 pages. The physical page for each token can be looked up from the pre-loaded page array. For the K load: each token has K[phys_page, slot, hkv, :]. Since different tokens may be in different physical pages, this is a gather operation. The memory access pattern is not contiguous across tokens. But within a single token, the D elements of K are contiguous, so the per-token access is coalesced. The issue is that consecutive tokens may be on different physical pages, leading to cache misses. For BLOCK_SEQ = 16 (one page), all tokens are on the same physical page, so the K access is contiguous in the slot dimension: K[phys_page, 0:16, hkv, :]. This is a nice 2D contiguous access. For BLOCK_SEQ > 16, the access straddles page boundaries and becomes non-contiguous. So BLOCK_SEQ = page_size = 16 is optimal for memory access patterns. Going larger would hurt. For the GQA kernel with GROUP=8: - Each program loads Q for 8 heads (8 * 128 * 4 = 4 KB) - Iterates over 256 pages, loading K and V for each - For each page, does 8 QK^T computations - Accumulates 8 separate softmax states The total register usage per program: - Q: 8 * 128 = 1024 fp32 - K: 16 * 128 = 2048 fp32 - V: 16 * 128 = 2048 fp32 - mi: 8 fp32 - li: 8 fp32 - oi: 8 * 128 = 1024 fp32 - Intermediates (scores, es, etc.): ~16 + 16 + 128 = 160 fp32 - Total: ~5312 fp32 = 21 KB With 128 threads (4 warps): 42 fp32 per thread = 42 registers. Feasible (max 255). But with 256 threads (8 warps): 21 fp32 per thread. Even better. Actually, I realize that Triton distributes tensors across threads differently. A tensor of shape (16, 128) with 128 threads: Triton might assign 1 row per thread (16 elements per thread * 16 rows = 256 total iterations). Or it might assign elements across all threads. The exact distribution depends on Triton's internal logic. But the key point is that the register usage is per-thread, and Triton tries to minimize it. OK, let me just write the code and test it. I'll use num_warps=8 for GROUP=8 and num_warps=4 for GROUP=4. Here's the final plan for the GQA kernel: ```python @triton.jit def _paged_attn_gqa_kernel( q_ptr, kv_ptr, bt_ptr, sl_ptr, out_ptr, H: tl.constexpr, Hkv: tl.constexpr, D: tl.constexpr, P: tl.constexpr, GROUP: tl.constexpr, stride_qb, stride_qh, stride_kv0, stride_kv1, stride_kv2, stride_bt0, scale, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_SEQ: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) b = pid // Hkv hkv = pid % Hkv L = tl.load(sl_ptr + b).to(tl.int32) offs_d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D) mask_d = offs_d < D if L == 0: for g in range(GROUP): h = hkv * GROUP + g tl.store(out_ptr + b * stride_qb + h * stride_qh + offs_d, tl.zeros([BLOCK_D], tl.bfloat16), mask=mask_d) return # Load all query heads for this kv_head q = tl.zeros([GROUP, BLOCK_D], tl.float32) for g in range(GROUP): h = hkv * GROUP + g q[g] = tl.load(q_ptr + b * stride_qb + h * stride_qh + offs_d, mask=mask_d, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Accumulators mi = tl.full([GROUP], float('-inf'), tl.float32) li = tl.zeros([GROUP], tl.float32) oi = tl.zeros([GROUP, BLOCK_D], tl.float32) offs_s = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SEQ) npages = (L + P - 1) // P for pi in range(npages): pp = tl.load(bt_ptr + b * stride_bt0 + pi).to(tl.int32) toffs = pi * P + offs_s tmask = toffs < L # Load K, V once for this page (shared across all query heads) k_ptrs = (kv_ptr + pp * stride_kv0 + offs_s[:, None] * stride_kv1 + hkv * stride_kv2 + offs_d[None, :]) km = tmask[:, None] & mask_d[None, :] k = tl.load(k_ptrs, mask=km, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) v_ptrs = k_ptrs + D v = tl.load(v_ptrs, mask=km, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Process each query head for g in range(GROUP): # QK^T scores = tl.sum(q[g][None, :] * k, axis=1) * scale scores = tl.where(tmask, scores, float('-inf')) # Online softmax update mj = tl.max(scores) mnew = tl.maximum(mi[g], mj) corr = tl.exp(mi[g] - mnew) es = tl.exp(scores - mnew) es = tl.where(tmask, es, 0.0) li[g] = li[g] * corr + tl.sum(es) oi[g] = oi[g] * corr + tl.sum(es[:, None] * v, axis=0) mi[g] = mnew # Store outputs for g in range(GROUP): h = hkv * GROUP + g out_g = oi[g] * (1.0 / li[g]) tl.store(out_ptr + b * stride_qb + h * stride_qh + offs_d, out_g.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask_d) ``` This looks clean. When GROUP is a constexpr, `for g in range(GROUP)` will be unrolled. For GROUP=8, this creates 8 copies of the inner loop body. Each copy has: - 1 QK^T computation: sum over BLOCK_D - 1 softmax update: max, exp, sum - 1 weighted V accumulation: sum over BLOCK_SEQ The per-copy compute is small. The total compute for GROUP=8 per page is: - 8 * (128 muls + 16 muls + 128 muls) ≈ 2176 flops - Plus 8 * softmax operations This is negligible compared to the memory load (8 KB per page). Let me write a CUDA C++ kernel that: 1. Uses warp-level primitives for the dot product 2. Processes one (batch, query_head) per warp 3. Multiple warps per block for efficiency This avoids the Triton tensor indexing issue and gives me full control. For the CUDA kernel: ```cpp template <int D, int WARPS_PER_BLOCK> __global__ void paged_attn_decode_kernel(...) { int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; int global_warp = blockIdx.x * WARPS_PER_BLOCK + warp_id; if (global_warp >= batch * num_heads) return; int b = global_warp / num_heads; int h = global_warp % num_heads; int hkv = h / group_size; int seq_len = seq_lens[b]; float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)D); constexpr int ELEMS = D / 32; // elements per lane // Load Q const __nv_bfloat16* q_ptr = query + (b * num_heads + h) * D; float q[ELEMS]; for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { q[i] = __bfloat162float(q_ptr[lane_id * ELEMS + i]); } // Online softmax accumulators float mi = -FLT_MAX; float li = 0.0f; float oi[ELEMS] = {0.0f}; int num_pages = (seq_len + page_size - 1) / page_size; for (int p = 0; p < num_pages; p++) { int phys_page = block_table[b * max_pages + p]; int page_start = p * page_size; int page_end = min(page_start + page_size, seq_len); for (int s = page_start; s < page_end; s++) { int slot = s - page_start; // Pointer to K for this token const __nv_bfloat16* kv_base = kv_cache + (size_t)phys_page * page_size * num_kv_heads * D * 2 + slot * num_kv_heads * D * 2 + hkv * D * 2; // Load K and compute QK^T float dot = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { float ki = __bfloat162float(kv_base[lane_id * ELEMS + i]); dot += q[i] * ki; } // Warp reduce float score = warp_reduce_sum(dot) * scale; // Online softmax update float mnew = fmaxf(mi, score); float corr = expf(mi - mnew); float es = expf(score - mnew); li = li * corr + es; for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { oi[i] *= corr; } // Load V and accumulate const __nv_bfloat16* v_base = kv_base + D; for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { float vi = __bfloat162float(v_base[lane_id * ELEMS + i]); oi[i] += es * vi; } mi = mnew; } } // Normalize and store __nv_bfloat16* out_ptr = output + (b * num_heads + h) * D; float inv_li = 1.0f / li; for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { out_ptr[lane_id * ELEMS + i] = __float2bfloat16(oi[i] * inv_li); } } ``` This is clean, simple, and efficient. Each warp handles one (b, h) independently. The dot product uses warp shuffle for reduction. The GQA optimization is implicit: when multiple warps in the same block handle the same (b, hkv) but different query heads, they all read the same K and V data. The L2 cache should handle the sharing. For explicit GQA sharing in CUDA C++, I could use shared memory: 1. One warp loads K and V into shared memory 2. All GROUP warps use the shared K and V 3. Synchronize and move to the next page This would be more efficient because shared memory is much faster than global memory. But it requires more complex coordination. Let me first try the simple CUDA kernel (no GQA sharing, relying on L2 cache) and see how it performs. If needed, I'll add shared memory GQA sharing. Actually, the Triton kernel already achieves the same thing as the simple CUDA kernel (one warp per (b, h)). The CUDA kernel might be slightly faster due to direct warp control, but the difference should be small. Let me think about what would give the biggest performance improvement: 1. **GQA sharing via shared memory** (CUDA C++): Load K, V into shared memory, share across warps 2. **Better memory access pattern**: Pack K and V into one load 3. **More parallelism**: Split sequence into tiles I think option 1 (GQA sharing via shared memory in CUDA C++) would give the biggest improvement because: - Shared memory bandwidth is ~19 TB/s (vs 1.8 TB/s DRAM) - K and V are loaded once per page instead of GROUP times - The total DRAM traffic drops to the unique data size Let me implement this. **CUDA C++ GQA kernel with shared memory:** Block layout: (WARPS_PER_BLOCK * 32,) threads Each warp handles one (b, h). Within a block, all warps handle the same batch element b and same kv_head hkv. Wait, to share K and V via shared memory, the warps in a block need to process the same kv_head. This means I need to group warps by (b, hkv). Grid: (B * Hkv * page_blocks, 1, 1) where page_blocks tiles the sequence Block: (GROUP * 32,) threads where each warp handles one query head Wait, this is getting complicated. Let me think more carefully. **Design: One block per (batch, kv_head), GROUP warps per block** - Grid: (B * Hkv,) - Block: (GROUP * 32,) = (group_size * 32) threads - Warp g handles query head hkv * GROUP + g - All warps share K and V via shared memory For each page: 1. Warp 0 loads K into shared memory (or all warps cooperatively load K) 2. __syncthreads() 3. Each warp computes its QK^T from shared K 4. Warp 0 loads V into shared memory (or cooperative) 5. __syncthreads() 6. Each warp computes its weighted V from shared V 7. __syncthreads() (to ensure all warps are done with shared memory before next page) Actually, loading K and V cooperatively is more efficient: - K is (P, D) bf16 = (16, 128) * 2 = 4 KB - V is same = 4 KB - Total shared memory per page: 8 KB - With GROUP=4 warps = 128 threads, each thread loads 4 KB / 128 = 32 bytes. That's 16 bf16 elements per thread. For D=128, P=16: K is 16 * 128 = 2048 bf16 elements. With 128 threads, each loads 16 elements. But the access pattern matters: we want coalesced loads. The K data in global memory is at: kv_cache[phys_page, :, hkv, :D] = kv_cache + phys_page * P * Hkv * 2D + hkv * 2D For coalesced access, consecutive threads should access consecutive addresses. If thread t loads K[t/128 * 16 + (t%128)] or similar... Actually, for the cooperative load: - Thread 0 loads K[0, 0:15], thread 1 loads K[0, 16:31], ..., thread 7 loads K[0, 112:127] - Thread 8 loads K[1, 0:15], ... - This gives coalesced access because consecutive threads access consecutive K elements Wait, that's not quite right. K is stored as (P, Hkv, 2D), so K[s, hkv, :] is at a contiguous range of 2D elements. For loading K[s, hkv, :D]: - 128 threads, 2048 elements - Each thread loads 16 elements - Thread t loads elements [t*16 : t*16+16] from the flat K array - If t*16 to t*16+16 spans multiple rows (P=16, D=128: each row is 128 elements) - Thread 0: elements 0-15 (row 0, cols 0-15) - Thread 1: elements 16-31 (row 0, cols 16-31) - ... - Thread 7: elements 112-127 (row 0, cols 112-127) - Thread 8: elements 128-143 (row 1, cols 0-15) - ... This gives coalesced access because consecutive threads access consecutive addresses within a row. But wait, the K data in global memory is not contiguous in the way I assumed. The layout is (num_blocks, page_size, num_kv_heads, 2*D). For a given phys_page and hkv: K[s, d] = kv_cache[phys_page, s, hkv, d] The stride between consecutive d values is 1 (contiguous in the last dimension). The stride between consecutive s values is Hkv * 2D. The stride between consecutive hkv values is 2D. So K[s, hkv, :] is at addresses: kv_cache + phys_page * P * Hkv * 2D + s * Hkv * 2D + hkv * 2D + d For a warp of 32 threads loading a row of K (D=128 bf16): - Thread 0: K[s, hkv, 0:3] (4 bf16 = 8 bytes) - Thread 1: K[s, hkv, 4:7] - ... - Thread 31: K[s, hkv, 124:127] These are consecutive in memory, so perfectly coalesced. For the cooperative load by 128 threads (4 warps): - I need to load P * D = 16 * 128 = 2048 bf16 elements - 128 threads, each loads 16 bf16 - Thread t loads: K[s, hkv, d:d+15] where s and d are derived from t To maximize coalescing: - Map threads to (s, d) pairs where d varies fastest - s = t / (D/16) = t / 8 - d = (t % 8) * 16 Wait, this is getting complicated. Let me just use a simpler approach: Load K and V per-token instead of per-page. For each token, a single warp (32 threads) loads K[D] and V[D] coalesced. The warp computes its QK^T and accumulates. For the GQA sharing, all GROUP warps process the same token but different query heads. Since they all load the same K and V, the L2 cache should share the data. But I want to use shared memory for guaranteed sharing. Here's the approach: For each token: 1. One warp loads K[D] into shared memory (128 bf16 = 256 bytes) 2. __syncwarp() (or __syncthreads if needed) 3. All GROUP warps read K from shared memory and compute their QK^T 4. One warp loads V[D] into shared memory 5. All GROUP warps read V from shared memory But loading K[D] = 128 bf16 = 256 bytes by one warp (32 threads): each thread loads 4 bf16. Coalesced. Hmm, but which warp does the loading? And how do we synchronize between warps? Actually, for this approach, I need __syncthreads() between loading K/V and using them, which is expensive. Alternative: all warps cooperatively load K and V. Each warp loads a portion: - GROUP warps, each loads D/GROUP elements - For D=128, GROUP=4: each warp loads 32 bf16 = 64 bytes But the coalescing might not be great if different warps load non-adjacent elements. Actually, let me think about this differently. The shared memory approach adds complexity but might not be necessary if the L2 cache already handles the sharing effectively. GQA kernel (explicit sharing): reads KV once per (b, hkv) If the L2 cache effectively shares KV data, the simple kernel's performance should be close to the GQA kernel's. For the kernel design, I'll use the following approach: **Approach: Warp-per-head with cooperative page loading** Block: (WARPS_PER_BLOCK * 32,) threads Grid: (B * H / WARPS_PER_BLOCK,) blocks Each warp handles one (b, h) independently. The warp: 1. Loads Q into registers 2. Iterates over pages 3. For each page, loads K and V (coalesced warp-level loads) 4. Computes online softmax No shared memory, no GQA sharing. Relies on L2 cache for GQA sharing. This is the simplest CUDA approach. Let me implement it. For SM120 (Blackwell), I should compile with -gencode=arch=compute_120,code=sm_120 or similar. Actually, load_inline with PyTorch should automatically detect the right GPU architecture. Let me not worry about this. Let me write the kernel code now. ```cpp #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_bf16.h> #include <cfloat> #include <math.h> static const int WARP_SIZE = 32; __device__ __forceinline__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int mask = 16; mask > 0; mask >>= 1) { val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, val, mask); } return val; } template <int HEAD_DIM, int WARPS_PER_BLOCK> __global__ void __launch_bounds__(WARPS_PER_BLOCK * 32) paged_attn_decode_kernel( const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ query, // (B, H, D) const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ kv_cache, // (num_blocks, P, Hkv, 2*D) const int* __restrict__ block_table, // (B, max_pages) const int* __restrict__ seq_lens, // (B,) __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ output, // (B, H, D) int batch, int num_heads, int num_kv_heads, int page_size, int max_pages, int group_size ) { const int warp_id = threadIdx.x / WARP_SIZE; const int lane_id = threadIdx.x % WARP_SIZE; const int global_warp = blockIdx.x * WARPS_PER_BLOCK + warp_id; const int total_warps = batch * num_heads; if (global_warp >= total_warps) return; const int b = global_warp / num_heads; const int h = global_warp % num_heads; const int hkv = h / group_size; const int seq_len = seq_lens[b]; if (seq_len == 0) { const __nv_bfloat16* q_base = query + (size_t)(b * num_heads + h) * HEAD_DIM; __nv_bfloat16* o_base = output + (size_t)(b * num_heads + h) * HEAD_DIM; constexpr int ELEMS = HEAD_DIM / WARP_SIZE; for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { o_base[lane_id * ELEMS + i] = __float2bfloat16(0.0f); } return; } const float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)HEAD_DIM); constexpr int ELEMS = HEAD_DIM / WARP_SIZE; // Load Q const __nv_bfloat16* q_base = query + (size_t)(b * num_heads + h) * HEAD_DIM; float q[ELEMS]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { q[i] = __bfloat162float(q_base[lane_id * ELEMS + i]); } // Accumulators float mi = -FLT_MAX; float li = 0.0f; float oi[ELEMS]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) oi[i] = 0.0f; // Strides for KV cache: (num_blocks, P, Hkv, 2*D) const size_t kv_block_stride = (size_t)page_size * num_kv_heads * HEAD_DIM * 2; const size_t kv_slot_stride = (size_t)num_kv_heads * HEAD_DIM * 2; const size_t kv_head_stride = (size_t)HEAD_DIM * 2; const int num_pages = (seq_len + page_size - 1) / page_size; for (int p = 0; p < num_pages; p++) { const int phys_page = block_table[b * max_pages + p]; const int page_start = p * page_size; const int page_end = min(page_start + page_size, seq_len); const __nv_bfloat16* kv_page_base = kv_cache + (size_t)phys_page * kv_block_stride + (size_t)hkv * kv_head_stride; for (int s = page_start; s < page_end; s++) { const int slot = s - page_start; const __nv_bfloat16* kv_row = kv_page_base + (size_t)slot * kv_slot_stride; // Load K and compute QK^T float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { float ki = __bfloat162float(kv_row[lane_id * ELEMS + i]); dot += q[i] * ki; } float score = warp_reduce_sum(dot) * scale; // Online softmax update float mnew = fmaxf(mi, score); float corr = expf(mi - mnew); float es = expf(score - mnew); li = li * corr + es; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { oi[i] *= corr; } // Load V and accumulate const __nv_bfloat16* v_row = kv_row + HEAD_DIM; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { float vi = __bfloat162float(v_row[lane_id * ELEMS + i]); oi[i] += es * vi; } mi = mnew; } } // Normalize and store __nv_bfloat16* o_base = output + (size_t)(b * num_heads + h) * HEAD_DIM; float inv_li = 1.0f / li; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { o_base[lane_id * ELEMS + i] = __float2bfloat16(oi[i] * inv_li); } } ``` This is clean. Now let me think about how to dispatch this kernel from PyTorch. For load_inline, I need: 1. The C++ kernel code 2. A wrapper function that's callable from Python 3. Template instantiations for different HEAD_DIM values Wait, load_inline compiles the code at import time. I need to provide the kernel code and the Python binding. Let me use PyTorch's cpp_extension load_inline: ```python from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = "..." module = load_inline( name="paged_attn_cuda", cpp_sources="", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["paged_attn_decode_launcher"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "-std=c++17"], ) ``` The launcher function dispatches to the right template instantiation based on head_dim. Let me write the full CUDA source with the launcher: ```cpp #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_bf16.h> #include <cfloat> #include <math.h> #include <torch/extension.h> // ... kernel code ... torch::Tensor paged_attn_decode_launcher( torch::Tensor query, // (B, H, D) bf16 torch::Tensor kv_cache, // (num_blocks, P, Hkv, 2*D) bf16 torch::Tensor block_table, // (B, max_pages) int32 torch::Tensor seq_lens // (B,) int32 ) { auto B = query.size(0); auto H = query.size(1); auto D = query.size(2); auto Hkv = kv_cache.size(2); auto P = kv_cache.size(1); int group_size = H / Hkv; int max_pages = block_table.size(1); auto output = torch::empty_like(query); int total_warps = B * H; constexpr int WARPS_PER_BLOCK = 4; int blocks = (total_warps + WARPS_PER_BLOCK - 1) / WARPS_PER_BLOCK; int threads = WARPS_PER_BLOCK * 32; if (D == 128) { paged_attn_decode_kernel<128, WARPS_PER_BLOCK><<<blocks, threads>>>( query.data_ptr<__nv_bfloat16>(), kv_cache.data_ptr<__nv_bfloat16>(), block_table.data_ptr<int>(), seq_lens.data_ptr<int>(), output.data_ptr<__nv_bfloat16>(), B, H, Hkv, P, max_pages, group_size ); } else if (D == 64) { paged_attn_decode_kernel<64, WARPS_PER_BLOCK><<<blocks, threads>>>( query.data_ptr<__nv_bfloat16>(), kv_cache.data_ptr<__nv_bfloat16>(), block_table.data_ptr<int>(), seq_lens.data_ptr<int>(), output.data_ptr<__nv_bfloat16>(), B, H, Hkv, P, max_pages, group_size ); } return output; } ``` Wait, I should also handle the SM architecture. For SM120 (Blackwell), nvcc from CUDA 13.2 should support -arch=sm_120. But before that, let me think about one more optimization: can I process K and V together? In the current kernel, I load K elements (ELEMS per thread), compute the dot product, then load V elements. This means two separate global memory loads per token. If I load K and V together (2*HEAD_DIM elements per thread, 2*ELEMS per thread), I can reduce to one load. But I need to store the V elements somewhere while computing the dot product. ```cpp // Load K and V together float k[ELEMS], v[ELEMS]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { __nv_bfloat16 kv_k = kv_row[lane_id * ELEMS + i]; __nv_bfloat16 kv_v = kv_row[HEAD_DIM + lane_id * ELEMS + i]; k[i] = __bfloat162float(kv_k); v[i] = __bfloat162float(kv_v); } ``` But K and V are not contiguous in memory (K is at offset 0, V is at offset HEAD_DIM), so this is two separate loads anyway. Let me try the following optimizations to the Triton kernel: 1. **Pack K and V into one load**: Load the full (BLOCK_SEQ, 2*D) packed KV and split 2. **Vectorized page table load**: Load all page numbers at once 3. **Better loop structure**: Process multiple pages per iteration Wait, actually, I just realized something. The current Triton kernel processes one page at a time (BLOCK_SEQ = page_size = 16). For L=4096, that's 256 loop iterations. Each iteration loads K and V (8 KB total) and does a small computation. The issue might be that the loop overhead is significant relative to the per-iteration work. Each iteration: - 1 block_table load (4 bytes) - 1 K load (4 KB) - 1 V load (4 KB) - 1 dot product (128 muls + 16 reductions) - 1 softmax update (max, exp, sum) - 1 weighted V accumulation (16 * 128 muls + 128 reductions) The memory loads are 8 KB per iteration. At 1800 GB/s, this takes ~4.4 ns per iteration. But the compute and loop overhead add more time. With 256 iterations, the total is at least 256 * 4.4 = 1127 ns ≈ 1.1 us. But the measured time for shape 2 is 409 us, which is 370x the theoretical minimum. So the overhead is huge. The overhead is likely from: 1. Triton's loop code generation (not as tight as hand-written CUDA) 2. Memory latency not fully hidden (not enough outstanding loads) 3. Kernel launch overhead 4. Low occupancy for shapes with few programs Let me try the CUDA C++ approach. It should have much lower overhead. Let me write the CUDA C++ kernel now. I'll keep it simple: one warp per (b, h), no GQA sharing. Actually, you know what, let me just try both approaches in parallel. I'll write a CUDA C++ version and see if it compiles and runs. If it does, I'll use it. If not, I'll fall back to optimizing the Triton kernel. Let me write the CUDA version now. One thing I want to do differently in the CUDA version: process tokens in the inner loop more efficiently. Instead of loading K and V for one token at a time, I can process multiple tokens. For D=128 with 32 threads per warp: each thread handles 4 elements. Each token requires: - K load: 4 bf16 per thread = 8 bytes per thread - Dot product: 4 muls + warp shuffle - V load: 4 bf16 per thread = 8 bytes per thread - Accumulation: 4 muls Total per token: 16 bytes loaded, 8 muls, 1 shuffle reduce. For page_size=16: 16 tokens per page. Per page: 256 bytes loaded, 128 muls, 16 shuffles. At 1800 GB/s: 256 bytes takes 0.14 ns. But each thread does 128 muls + 16 shuffles per page, which takes maybe 10-20 ns. So the compute dominates! Wait, that can't be right. Let me recalculate: - 16 tokens per page - Per token: 4 muls for K dot, 4 muls for V accum = 8 muls per thread - 16 tokens: 128 muls per thread - At ~1 GHz (CUDA core clock): 128 ns per page (rough estimate) - Warp shuffle: 16 shuffles * ~10 ns = 160 ns (this is overestimated) - Total per page: ~300 ns (rough) - 256 pages: 76 us But the measured time is 409 us. So there's ~5x overhead. This could be from: - Memory latency not hidden - Branching in the loop - Triton-specific overhead (for the Triton kernel) For CUDA C++, the overhead should be lower. Let me estimate: - 256 pages * 16 tokens * (16 bytes load + 8 muls) = 256 * 16 * ~20 ops = 81920 ops per thread - At ~4 GHz (boost clock): 81920 / 4e9 = 20 us - Plus memory: 256 * 16 * 32 bytes / 1800 GB/s = 131 KB / 1800 GB/s = 0.07 us (negligible) Wait, the memory access is not the bottleneck? Let me recalculate: - Per token: K load = 128 bf16 = 256 bytes, V load = 256 bytes - Per page: 16 * 512 = 8 KB - 256 pages: 2048 KB = 2 MB per (b, h) - At 1800 GB/s: 2 MB / 1800 GB/s = 1.1 us So the memory takes 1.1 us and the compute takes ~20 us. The kernel is actually compute-bound! But wait, the QK^T dot product is only D=128 multiply-adds. With 32 threads per warp, each thread does 4 muls. At 4 GHz: 4 muls / 4 GHz = 1 ns. Warp shuffle adds ~5-10 ns. Total per token: ~10-15 ns. 16 tokens per page: 160-240 ns. 256 pages: 41-61 us. Plus V accumulation: similar. Total: 82-122 us. Memory: 2 MB / 1800 GB/s = 1.1 us. So the kernel is definitely compute-bound for the per-warp view! But the GPU has many warps running concurrently. With 256 warps on 170 SMs, each SM runs ~1.5 warps. The SM can pipeline memory and compute across warps, effectively hiding memory latency. The total compute time is bounded by the SM throughput: - 256 warps * 256 pages * 16 tokens * 8 muls per thread = 256 * 256 * 16 * 8 * 4 = 33554432 muls per thread - Wait, that doesn't make sense. Let me recalculate. Per warp: 256 pages * 16 tokens * (4 K muls + 4 V muls) = 256 * 16 * 8 = 32768 muls per thread Per warp: 256 * 16 * (1 warp shuffle for score + 0 for accum) = 4096 shuffles Total threads: 256 * 32 = 8192 Total muls: 8192 * 32768 = 268M muls At 2 TFLOPS (bf16 on SM120? probably much higher): 268M / 2e12 = 0.13 us At 100 TFLOPS (SM120 dense bf16?): 268M / 100e12 = 0.003 us OK so the compute is negligible even on a slow GPU. The bottleneck must be elsewhere. Let me think about this more carefully. The warp shuffle operations have latency. Each token requires 1 warp shuffle (for the dot product reduction). With 16 tokens per page and 256 pages: 4096 shuffles per warp. Each shuffle takes ~10-20 cycles. At 2 GHz: 10 cycles * 4096 = 40960 cycles = 20 us. But shuffles can be pipelined with compute. The warp does the dot product reduction while also loading the next token's K. Actually, I think the main bottleneck for the per-warp execution is the sequential dependency in the online softmax. Each token's softmax update depends on the previous token's result (through mi, li, oi). This prevents parallelism across tokens. Wait, that's not entirely true. The online softmax has a dependency: the correction factor depends on the current running max, which depends on all previous tokens. So yes, it's sequential. But this sequential dependency only affects the online softmax accumulation, not the QK^T computation. The QK^T can be computed independently for each token. The softmax is applied after all QK^T scores are computed. Hmm, but in my kernel, I'm doing the online softmax per token (not per page). This means: 1. Load K for token s 2. Compute score = Q @ K_s 3. Update running max, sum, output 4. Load V for token s 5. Update output accumulation 6. Go to next token The dependency chain is: score_s -> update_s -> (load V_s -> accumulate_s) -> load K_{s+1} -> score_{s+1} -> ... This is a serial chain. The critical path per token is: - Load K: ~100-200 cycles (DRAM latency, ~50-100 ns) - Compute dot product: ~10 cycles - Warp reduce: ~10 cycles - Compute softmax update: ~10 cycles - Load V: ~100-200 cycles (DRAM latency) - Accumulate V: ~10 cycles Total per token: ~300-500 cycles = 150-250 ns But this assumes the loads are from DRAM. If the K and V data is in L2 cache (~20-50 ns latency), the per-token time drops to ~50-100 ns. For 4096 tokens: 4096 * 100 ns = 410 us. That matches the measured 409 us! So the bottleneck is memory latency, not bandwidth. Each token requires a dependent load of K and V, and the serial dependency chain prevents latency hiding within a single warp. To hide memory latency, I need more concurrent warps. With enough warps, the GPU can interleave their memory accesses, keeping the memory pipeline busy while individual warps wait for their data. The key insight: for shape 2, we have 256 warps (B=4, H=64). Each warp processes 4096 tokens sequentially. The GPU switches between warps while they wait for memory. With 256 warps and 170 SMs, each SM has ~1.5 warps. This is not enough to hide memory latency. To increase the number of warps, I need more (B, H) pairs or more parallelism within each pair. For shape 2: B=4, H=64. The maximum warps = 4 * 64 = 256. With 170 SMs, that's 1.5 warps per SM. To increase warps per SM, I need to either: 1. Increase batch size or number of heads (can't change) 2. Process each (b, h) pair with multiple warps (split the sequence) Option 2 is the way to go. If I split the sequence of each (b, h) into T tiles, each tile is processed by a separate warp. The total warps = B * H * T. For T=16: 4 * 64 * 16 = 4096 warps. With 170 SMs: 24 warps per SM. Much better! After all tiles finish, a reduction kernel combines the partial results. This is the sequence-tiling approach I considered earlier. Let me implement it. **Two-pass approach:** Pass 1: Partial attention - Grid: (B * H * T,) where T = number of sequence tiles - Each program handles B * H * T pages (one tile of the sequence) - Outputs partial (mi, li, oi) for each (b, h, tile) Pass 2: Reduction - Grid: (B * H,) - Each program reduces T partial results into a single output - Combines (mi, li, oi) using the online softmax merge formula For the partial results, I need a scratch buffer of size: - B * H * T * (1 + 1 + D) floats = B * H * T * (D + 2) * 4 bytes - For shape 2: 4 * 64 * 16 * (128 + 2) * 4 = 4 * 64 * 16 * 130 * 4 = 2.1 MB This fits easily in GPU memory. Let me implement this two-pass approach. Actually, for the Triton implementation, I need to be careful about how I store the partial results. The layout should be: - partial_m: (B, H, T) float - partial_l: (B, H, T) float - partial_o: (B, H, T, D) float Then the reduction kernel reads these and produces the final output. Let me implement this. I'll use Triton for both passes. Actually, for the reduction pass, I need to handle the case where different tiles have different numbers of valid tokens (the last tile might be partial). But since I'm using the online softmax formulation, the partial results are already normalized per tile (mi, li, oi where oi is the unnormalized output for that tile). The reduction just merges them. Wait, in the first pass, each tile produces: - mi_tile: max score in the tile - li_tile: sum of exp(score - mi_tile) for scores in the tile - oi_tile: sum of exp(score - mi_tile) * V for scores in the tile (unnormalized) These are the standard FlashAttention partial results. The merge is: - m_global = max(mi_1, mi_2, ..., mi_T) - l_global = sum(li_t * exp(mi_t - m_global)) - o_global = sum(oi_t * exp(mi_t - m_global)) - Final: o_global / l_global This is correct. For the tile size: each tile processes ceil(num_pages / T) pages. If num_pages is not divisible by T, the last tile has fewer pages. The tile with 0 valid pages should produce mi=-inf, li=0, oi=zeros. Let me implement this. For the Triton kernel, the first pass: ```python @triton.jit def _paged_attn_decode_tiled_kernel( q_ptr, kv_ptr, bt_ptr, sl_ptr, partial_m_ptr, partial_l_ptr, partial_o_ptr, H: tl.constexpr, Hkv: tl.constexpr, D: tl.constexpr, P: tl.constexpr, stride_qb, stride_qh, stride_kv0, stride_kv1, stride_kv2, stride_bt0, stride_pm_bh, stride_pm_t, stride_po_bh, stride_po_t, stride_po_d, scale, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_SEQ: tl.constexpr, NUM_TILES: tl.constexpr, PAGES_PER_TILE: tl.constexpr, # ceil(num_pages / NUM_TILES) ): pid = tl.program_id(0) bh = pid // NUM_TILES tile = pid % NUM_TILES b = bh // H h = bh % H group = H // Hkv hkv = h // group L = tl.load(sl_ptr + b).to(tl.int32) offs_d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D) mask_d = offs_d < D if L == 0: # Store empty partial result tl.store(partial_m_ptr + bh * stride_pm_bh + tile * stride_pm_t, float('-inf')) tl.store(partial_l_ptr + bh * stride_pm_bh + tile * stride_pm_t, 0.0) tl.store(partial_o_ptr + bh * stride_po_bh + tile * stride_po_t + offs_d, tl.zeros([BLOCK_D], tl.float32), mask=mask_d) return # Load Q q = tl.load(q_ptr + b * stride_qb + h * stride_qh + offs_d, mask=mask_d, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) mi = float('-inf') li = float(0.0) oi = tl.zeros([BLOCK_D], tl.float32) offs_s = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_SEQ) page_start = tile * PAGES_PER_TILE * P page_end = min((tile + 1) * PAGES_PER_TILE * P, L) tile_pages_start = tile * PAGES_PER_TILE tile_pages_end = min((tile + 1) * PAGES_PER_TILE, (L + P - 1) // P) for pi in range(tile_pages_start, tile_pages_end): pp = tl.load(bt_ptr + b * stride_bt0 + pi).to(tl.int32) toffs = pi * P + offs_s tmask = toffs < L k_ptrs = (kv_ptr + pp * stride_kv0 + offs_s[:, None] * stride_kv1 + hkv * stride_kv2 + offs_d[None, :]) km = tmask[:, None] & mask_d[None, :] k = tl.load(k_ptrs, mask=km, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) s = tl.sum(q[None, :] * k, axis=1) * scale s = tl.where(tmask, s, float('-inf')) mj = tl.max(s) mnew = tl.maximum(mi, mj) corr = tl.exp(mi - mnew) es = tl.exp(s - mnew) es = tl.where(tmask, es, 0.0) li = li * corr + tl.sum(es) oi = oi * corr v_ptrs = k_ptrs + D v = tl.load(v_ptrs, mask=km, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) oi += tl.sum(es[:, None] * v, axis=0) mi = mnew # Store partial results tl.store(partial_m_ptr + bh * stride_pm_bh + tile * stride_pm_t, mi) tl.store(partial_l_ptr + bh * stride_pm_bh + tile * stride_pm_t, li) tl.store(partial_o_ptr + bh * stride_po_bh + tile * stride_po_t + offs_d, oi, mask=mask_d) ``` And the reduction kernel: ```python @triton.jit def _paged_attn_reduce_kernel( partial_m_ptr, partial_l_ptr, partial_o_ptr, out_ptr, H: tl.constexpr, D: tl.constexpr, stride_ob, stride_oh, stride_pm_bh, stride_pm_t, stride_pl_bh, stride_pl_t, stride_po_bh, stride_po_t, stride_po_d, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr, NUM_TILES: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) b = pid // H h = pid % H offs_d = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D) mask_d = offs_d < D offs_t = tl.arange(0, NUM_TILES) # Load partial results: (NUM_TILES,) for m and l, (NUM_TILES, BLOCK_D) for o m = tl.load(partial_m_ptr + pid * stride_pm_bh + offs_t * stride_pm_t) l = tl.load(partial_l_ptr + pid * stride_pl_bh + offs_t * stride_pl_t) o = tl.load(partial_o_ptr + pid * stride_po_bh + offs_t[:, None] * stride_po_t + offs_d[None, :], mask=mask_d[None, :], other=0.0) # Reduce m_global = tl.max(m) correction = tl.exp(m - m_global) # (NUM_TILES,) l_global = tl.sum(l * correction) # Rescale and sum o o_global = tl.sum(o * correction[:, None], axis=0) # (BLOCK_D,) # Normalize o_final = o_global / l_global # Store tl.store(out_ptr + b * stride_ob + h * stride_oh + offs_d, o_final.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=mask_d) ``` This is a clean two-pass approach. The key advantage is that the first pass has B * H * T programs, which is T times more parallelism than the single-pass approach. For shape 2 with T=16: 4 * 64 * 16 = 4096 programs. With 4 warps each: 16384 warps. For 170 SMs: 96 warps per SM. This is much better! Let me implement this in solution.py and test it. But wait, there's a complication: PAGES_PER_TILE is a constexpr, but it depends on num_pages, which depends on seq_len. seq_len is different for each batch element. But in our shapes, all batch elements have the same seq_len. So num_pages is the same for all. For shapes with uniform seq_len (all our shapes), PAGES_PER_TILE = ceil(num_pages / T). This can be computed at kernel launch time. But what about the case where num_pages is not divisible by T? The last tile has fewer pages. The kernel handles this by checking `pi < tile_pages_end` and `toffs < L`. Actually, looking at my kernel, I iterate from `tile_pages_start` to `tile_pages_end`, where `tile_pages_end = min((tile + 1) * PAGES_PER_TILE, total_pages)`. This correctly handles the last tile. But there's a subtlety: the loop bound `tile_pages_end` is a runtime value. In Triton, I need to compute this inside the kernel: ```python total_pages = (L + P - 1) // P tile_pages_start = tile * PAGES_PER_TILE tile_pages_end = min((tile + 1) * PAGES_PER_TILE, total_pages) ``` This should work in Triton. But wait, if tile_pages_end <= tile_pages_start (tile has no pages), the loop doesn't execute, and the partial result is mi=-inf, li=0, oi=0. This is correct for the merge. OK, let me implement this. With the single-pass kernel, 256 programs is reasonable. The issue is that the serial online softmax within each program creates a long dependency chain. Hmm, let me reconsider. The latency issue is per-warp: each warp processes L tokens sequentially. The latency per token is dominated by memory access (~100 ns from L2, ~300 ns from DRAM). For L=4096 tokens per warp: 4096 * 100 ns = 410 us (with L2 caching) or 4096 * 300 ns = 1229 us (without). With T tiles: each warp processes L/T tokens. For T=16: 256 tokens per warp. Time: 256 * 100 = 25.6 us (with L2) or 256 * 300 = 76.8 us (without). With 4096 warps on 170 SMs: 24 warps per SM. The GPU can interleave their execution, hiding individual warp latency. The total time should be bounded by the slowest warp: ~76.8 us. Plus the reduction kernel: negligible. So the tiled approach should reduce the time from ~410 us to ~77 us, a ~5x improvement. But wait, the reduction adds overhead. Let me estimate: - Reduction kernel: B * H = 256 programs, each loading T * (D+2) floats = 16 * 130 * 4 = 8.3 KB - Total data: 256 * 8.3 KB = 2.1 MB - At 1800 GB/s: 2.1 MB / 1800 GB/s = 1.2 us - Plus compute: negligible So the reduction is ~1-5 us. The total tiled time: 77 + 5 = 82 us. This would give: - bytes = 67.1 MB - time = 82 us - bandwidth = 67.1 MB / 82 us = 818 GB/s - peak_fraction = 818 / 1800 = 0.454 That's a big improvement from 0.091! But I'm being optimistic about the per-warp latency. Let me be more realistic. Actually, the per-warp latency depends on the memory access pattern. If the KV data is in L2 cache, the latency is ~20-50 ns per access. If in DRAM, it's ~100-300 ns. For the tiled approach, each warp processes 256 tokens across 16 pages. The K and V data for each page is 8 KB. With 16 pages: 128 KB per warp. With 24 warps per SM, each SM needs 24 * 128 KB = 3 MB of L2 cache. If L2 is 96 MB, this fits. So the KV data should be in L2 cache after the first access. The per-token latency should be ~20-50 ns. 256 tokens * 30 ns = 7.7 us per warp. With 4096 warps on 170 SMs: 4096 / 170 = 24 concurrent warps per SM. Total time: ~7.7 us for the tiled pass + ~5 us for reduction = ~13 us. Peak fraction = 67.1 MB / 13 us / 1800 GB/s = 2.86. That's > 1.0, which is impossible! Let me recalculate. The total DRAM traffic for the tiled pass: - Each tile reads from a different portion of the KV cache - Total unique KV data: B * L * Hkv * D * 2 * 2 = 4 * 4096 * 8 * 128 * 2 * 2 = 67 MB - But with GROUP=8, each query head reads the same KV. With 64 query heads per batch, the KV for each kv_head is read 8 times per batch. - If the L2 caches KV within a batch element: 67 MB unique data - If not: 67 * 8 = 537 MB At 1800 GB/s: 67 MB / 1800 GB/s = 37 us (best case), 537 MB / 1800 GB/s = 298 us (worst case). My estimate of 13 us is too optimistic. The realistic time is 37-298 us. Let me aim for the best case (67 MB, L2 cached). With the tiled approach, each tile reads a different portion of the KV cache. For a given (b, hkv), all GROUP tiles read different pages. But across query heads (same b, same hkv), the tiles read the same pages. In the tiled kernel, programs are ordered as (b * H + h) * T + tile. For the same (b, hkv), programs with different h but same tile read the same KV pages. These programs might run on different SMs, so the L2 cache can share the data. With T=16 and GROUP=8: for each (b, hkv), there are GROUP * T = 128 programs, each reading different tiles. The KV data per (b, hkv, tile) is 2 * (L/T) * D * 2 = 2 * 256 * 128 * 2 = 128 KB. With 32 (b, hkv) pairs: 32 * 128 KB = 4 MB per tile. Across 16 tiles: 64 MB total. This fits in L2. But the timing of the accesses matters. If programs for the same (b, hkv) but different tiles run concurrently, they access different pages, so no sharing. If programs for the same (b, hkv, tile) but different h run concurrently, they access the same pages, and L2 can share. With the current grid ordering, programs are (b * H + h) * T + tile. So: - pid 0: (b=0, h=0, tile=0) - pid 1: (b=0, h=0, tile=1) - ... - pid 15: (b=0, h=0, tile=15) - pid 16: (b=0, h=1, tile=0) - pid 17: (b=0, h=1, tile=1) - ... For (b=0, hkv=0), the query heads are h=0,1,2,3 (GROUP=4 for most shapes). Their tiles: - pid 0-15: (b=0, h=0, tiles 0-15) -> hkv=0, pages vary - pid 16-31: (b=0, h=1, tiles 0-15) -> hkv=0, same pages as h=0 for same tile - pid 32-47: (b=0, h=2, tiles 0-15) -> hkv=0 - pid 48-63: (b=0, h=3, tiles 0-15) -> hkv=0 Programs for the same tile but different h: pid 0 and pid 16 (both tile 0) run at different times (pid 0 is scheduled first). By the time pid 16 runs, pid 0 might have already finished, and its KV data might have been evicted from L2. To improve L2 sharing, I should reorder the grid so that programs for the same (b, hkv, tile) but different h are adjacent: pid = tile * B * H + (b * H + h) Or more precisely, group by (b, hkv, tile) and iterate over h within the group. This way, programs for the same (b, hkv, tile) are adjacent (g varies), maximizing L2 cache hits. But this complicates the indexing. The current performance: - Shape 0: 0.146 (262 GB/s) - Shape 1: 0.329 (591 GB/s) - Shape 2: 0.091 (164 GB/s) - Shape 3: 0.261 (469 GB/s) - Shape 4: 0.058 (104 GB/s) - Geomean: 0.146 The benchmark counts unique KV bytes (using Hkv, not H). So the peak_fraction = achieved_bandwidth / 1800 GB/s, where achieved_bandwidth = unique_bytes / time. The time is determined by the actual DRAM traffic divided by the achieved DRAM bandwidth: time = actual_DRAM_traffic / achieved_DRAM_bandwidth The actual DRAM traffic = unique_bytes * redundancy_factor, where redundancy_factor depends on how well the L2 caches KV data across GQA heads. peak_fraction = unique_bytes / (time * 1800) = unique_bytes / (actual_DRAM_traffic / achieved_DRAM_bandwidth * 1800) = (unique_bytes / actual_DRAM_traffic) * (achieved_DRAM_bandwidth / 1800) If L2 perfectly caches: redundancy_factor = 1, peak_fraction = achieved_DRAM_bandwidth / 1800 If L2 doesn't cache: redundancy_factor = GROUP, peak_fraction = achieved_DRAM_bandwidth / (1800 * GROUP) For shape 1: peak_fraction = 0.329, GROUP=4. If no L2: 0.329 * 4 = 1.316. This exceeds 1.0, which means either L2 is caching (reducing redundancy) or the achieved DRAM bandwidth is > 1800 GB/s. Actually, the achieved DRAM bandwidth can be close to 1800 GB/s. Let me calculate: - Shape 1: time = 0.454 ms, unique_bytes = 256.5 MB - If redundancy = 1 (perfect L2): DRAM BW = 256.5 MB / 0.454 ms = 564 GB/s = 31.4% of peak - If redundancy = 2: DRAM BW = 2 * 256.5 / 0.454 = 1129 GB/s = 62.7% of peak - If redundancy = 4: DRAM BW = 4 * 256.5 / 0.454 = 2259 GB/s = 125% of peak (impossible!) So redundancy is between 1 and 3. If we assume achieved BW ≈ 80% of peak = 1440 GB/s: redundancy = 1440 * 0.454 / 256.5 = 2.55 So the L2 is caching about 1 - (2.55/4) = 36% of the redundant reads. Actually, let me take yet another approach. Let me just implement a simpler optimization that doesn't require two passes. **Optimization: Increase occupancy by using more warps per (b, h) pair** Instead of one warp per (b, h), use multiple warps that cooperatively process the same (b, h). Each warp handles a subset of the pages. This is essentially the tiled approach but within a single thread block. The advantage is that I can use shared memory for the reduction, avoiding a second kernel launch. Block: (TILES_PER_HEAD * 32,) threads Grid: (B * H,) Within each block: - TILES_PER_HEAD warps, each handling a different tile - After all warps finish, cooperatively reduce the partial results - Store the final output The shared memory is used for: - TILES_PER_HEAD * (1 + 1 + HEAD_DIM) floats = TILES_PER_HEAD * (HEAD_DIM + 2) * 4 bytes - For TILES=8, D=128: 8 * 130 * 4 = 4160 bytes ≈ 4 KB This fits easily in shared memory. The kernel flow: 1. Each warp loads its Q elements (already in registers) 2. Each warp iterates over its pages, computing partial (mi, li, oi) 3. __syncthreads() 4. Each warp writes its partial results to shared memory 5. __syncthreads() 6. One warp (or all warps cooperatively) reduces the partial results 7. Store final output For step 6, I can have warp 0 do the reduction: ```cpp if (warp_id == 0) { for (int t = 0; t < TILES_PER_HEAD; t++) { // Read partial_m[t], partial_l[t], partial_o[t] from shared memory // Merge with running accumulator } // Store final output } ``` This is clean and efficient. Let me implement this. But there's a subtlety: with TILES_PER_HEAD = 16, each warp processes num_pages / 16 pages. For shape 2 with 256 pages: 16 pages per warp. Each page has 16 tokens. Total per warp: 256 tokens. The per-warp execution time: 256 * ~30 ns = 7.7 us. With 16 warps per block, the block time is also ~7.7 us (warps run concurrently on the SM). Hmm, this means my time estimate is wrong. The actual time is bounded by the DRAM bandwidth: 537 MB / 1800 GB/s = 298 us. But my per-warp estimate was 7.7 us. With 16 warps per block and 1.5 blocks per SM, the warps are interleaved. The total time should be max(per-warp-latency * num-warp-batches, total-bytes / bandwidth). The bandwidth-bound time is 298 us. The latency-bound time is ~15 us. Since 298 >> 15, the kernel is bandwidth-bound. With GQA sharing (unique data only): 67.1 MB / 1800 GB/s = 37 us. The latency-bound time is still ~15 us. So with GQA sharing, the kernel would be bandwidth-bound at 37 us. This confirms that GQA sharing is critical. Without it, the bandwidth-bound time is 298 us (close to the current 409 us). With it, it's 37 us. But GQA sharing requires fewer blocks (B * Hkv instead of B * H). For shape 2: 32 blocks. With TILES=16: 32 blocks * 512 threads = 16384 threads. Per SM: 96 threads. With 16 warps per block: 32 * 16 = 512 warps. Per SM: 3 warps. That's low. To get more parallelism with GQA sharing, I need to increase TILES further. With TILES=64: 32 blocks * 2048 threads = 65536 threads. Per SM: 386 threads. Better. But the shared memory increases: 64 * 130 * 4 = 33 KB per block. With 96 KB per SM: 2.9 blocks per SM. 32 blocks / 170 SMs = 0.19 blocks per SM. Most SMs are idle. The fundamental problem is that B * Hkv = 32 is too few blocks. To solve this, I can separate the tiling and GQA sharing: 1. Grid: (B * Hkv * T,) blocks, each block handles one (b, hkv, tile) with GROUP warps 2. Each block loads K and V for its tile, shares across GROUP warps via shared memory 3. Each warp handles one query head 4. Partial results are written to global memory 5. A separate reduction kernel combines partial results This gives B * Hkv * T blocks. For shape 2 with T=16: 4 * 8 * 16 = 512 blocks. With GROUP=8 warps per block: 512 * 256 = 131072 threads. Per SM: 771 threads. Good! Shared memory per block: 2 * P * D * sizeof(bf16) = 2 * 16 * 128 * 2 = 8 KB (K and V for one page). Wait, each block handles TILES of pages, not one page. The shared memory only needs one page of K and V at a time (since we iterate over pages within the block). OK, shared memory = 2 * P * D * sizeof(bf16) = 8 KB per block. With 512 blocks and 170 SMs: 3 blocks per SM. 3 * 8 = 24 KB shared memory per SM (out of 96 KB). Fine. Each block has GROUP * 32 = 256 threads. 3 * 256 = 768 threads per SM. 37% occupancy. Decent. The per-block work: - pages_per_tile = 256 / 16 = 16 pages - Load K and V for each page into shared memory - Each of 8 warps processes one query head - Online softmax within each warp The per-block time: 16 pages * (load K+V to smem + 8 QK^T + 8 softmax + 8 V accum) ≈ 16 * 100 ns = 1.6 us (rough estimate). Total: 512 blocks, 3 blocks per SM, 170 SMs. Time = ceil(512/170) * 1.6 us = 3 * 1.6 = 4.8 us. DRAM traffic: 67.1 MB. Time: max(4.8 us, 67.1 MB / 1800 GB/s) = max(4.8, 37.3) = 37.3 us. Peak fraction: 67.1 MB / (37.3 us * 1800 GB/s) = 1.0. Perfect! But this is optimistic. The actual time will be higher due to: 1. Memory latency not fully hidden 2. Shared memory synchronization overhead 3. Reduction kernel overhead 4. Kernel launch overhead Realistically, I might get 50-100 us, which would give peak_fraction = 0.37 - 0.75. OK, this is a promising approach. Let me implement it. But this is quite complex: GQA sharing + shared memory + tiling + two-pass reduction. Let me estimate the code size: ~300-400 lines of CUDA. Doable but time-consuming. Let me think about whether there's a simpler approach that gets most of the benefit. **Simplest effective approach: CUDA C++ with warp-per-head, no tiling, no GQA sharing** This is the baseline CUDA kernel. It should be faster than the Triton kernel because of tighter code generation. The performance is limited by: 1. Low occupancy for shapes with few (B, H) pairs 2. Redundant KV reads (no GQA sharing) Expected performance: similar to the Triton kernel, maybe 1.5-2x faster. **Moderate approach: CUDA C++ with warp-per-head + tiling** Two-pass approach without GQA sharing. The tiling increases occupancy, which improves memory latency hiding. The L2 cache partially handles GQA sharing. Expected performance: 2-3x improvement over baseline. **Full approach: CUDA C++ with GQA sharing + shared memory + tiling** The most complex but potentially the fastest. All optimizations applied. Expected performance: 3-5x improvement over baseline. Let me go with the moderate approach first. If it's fast enough, I'm done. If not, I'll add GQA sharing. Actually, let me reconsider. The baseline Triton kernel gets geomean 0.146. I want to get to at least 0.3 (a 2x improvement). The CUDA C++ kernel might get 1.5-2x due to tighter code. That would put me at 0.22-0.29. Not quite enough. With tiling (no GQA sharing): 2-3x improvement. That's 0.29-0.44. This might be enough. With full optimizations: 3-5x. That's 0.44-0.73. Definitely enough. Let me go with the moderate approach (tiling, no GQA sharing). It's simpler than the full approach and should give a significant improvement. But actually, I realize the tiled approach requires two kernel launches (partial + reduction), which adds overhead. For the benchmark with 30 iterations, the overhead might be significant. Let me use the single-block approach with shared memory for reduction: - Grid: (B * H,) - Block: (TILES * 32,) - Shared memory for partial results - All TILES warps process the same (b, h) cooperatively - Reduce within the block using shared memory This avoids the second kernel launch. The grid is B * H, same as the original. For shape 2: B * H = 256 blocks. With TILES=4: 256 * 128 = 32768 threads. Per SM: 192. Good. For shape 4: B * H = 128 blocks. With TILES=8: 128 * 256 = 32768 threads. Per SM: 192. Good. This approach gives good occupancy with a single kernel launch. Shared memory per block: TILES * (1 + 1 + D) * 4 = TILES * (D + 2) * 4 bytes. For TILES=4, D=128: 4 * 130 * 4 = 2080 bytes ≈ 2 KB. Fine. For TILES=8, D=64: 8 * 66 * 4 = 2112 bytes ≈ 2 KB. Fine. OK, let me implement this approach. It's clean and efficient. Here's the CUDA kernel structure: ```cpp template <int HEAD_DIM, int TILES, int WARPS_PER_BLOCK = TILES> __global__ void __launch_bounds__(WARPS_PER_BLOCK * 32) paged_attn_tiled_kernel( const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ query, const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ kv_cache, const int* __restrict__ block_table, const int* __restrict__ seq_lens, __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ output, int B, int H, int Hkv, int P, int max_pages, int group_size, int pages_per_tile ) { // Shared memory for partial results // Layout: [TILES][HEAD_DIM + 2] floats // [t][0]: mi for tile t // [t][1]: li for tile t // [t][2..HEAD_DIM+1]: oi for tile t extern __shared__ float smem[]; const int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; const int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; const int b = blockIdx.x / H; const int h = blockIdx.x % H; const int hkv = h / group_size; const int seq_len = seq_lens[b]; if (seq_len == 0) { if (warp_id == 0) { __nv_bfloat16* o = output + (size_t)(b * H + h) * HEAD_DIM; constexpr int ELEMS = HEAD_DIM / 32; for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) o[lane_id * ELEMS + i] = __float2bfloat16(0.0f); } return; } const float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)HEAD_DIM); constexpr int ELEMS = HEAD_DIM / 32; // Load Q const __nv_bfloat16* q_base = query + (size_t)(b * H + h) * HEAD_DIM; float q[ELEMS]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) q[i] = __bfloat162float(q_base[lane_id * ELEMS + i]); // Each warp processes a tile of pages const int total_pages = (seq_len + P - 1) / P; const int my_tile_start = warp_id * pages_per_tile; const int my_tile_end = min(my_tile_start + pages_per_tile, total_pages); float mi = -FLT_MAX; float li = 0.0f; float oi[ELEMS]; memset(oi, 0, sizeof(oi)); // Strides const size_t kv_block_stride = (size_t)P * Hkv * HEAD_DIM * 2; const size_t kv_slot_stride = (size_t)Hkv * HEAD_DIM * 2; const size_t kv_head_stride = (size_t)HEAD_DIM * 2; for (int p = my_tile_start; p < my_tile_end; p++) { int phys_page = block_table[b * max_pages + p]; int page_start = p * P; int page_end = min(page_start + P, seq_len); const __nv_bfloat16* kv_base = kv_cache + (size_t)phys_page * kv_block_stride + (size_t)hkv * kv_head_stride; for (int s = page_start; s < page_end; s++) { int slot = s - page_start; const __nv_bfloat16* kv_row = kv_base + (size_t)slot * kv_slot_stride; // Load K and compute QK^T float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { float ki = __bfloat162float(kv_row[lane_id * ELEMS + i]); dot += q[i] * ki; } float score = warp_reduce_sum(dot) * scale; // Online softmax float mnew = fmaxf(mi, score); float corr = expf(mi - mnew); float es = expf(score - mnew); li = li * corr + es; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) oi[i] *= corr; // Load V const __nv_bfloat16* v_row = kv_row + HEAD_DIM; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { float vi = __bfloat162float(v_row[lane_id * ELEMS + i]); oi[i] += es * vi; } mi = mnew; } } // Write partial results to shared memory // smem layout: smem[tile * (HEAD_DIM + 2) + 0] = mi // smem[tile * (HEAD_DIM + 2) + 1] = li // smem[tile * (HEAD_DIM + 2) + 2 + lane * ELEMS + i] = oi[i] int smem_offset = warp_id * (HEAD_DIM + 2); if (lane_id == 0) { smem[smem_offset + 0] = mi; smem[smem_offset + 1] = li; } #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) smem[smem_offset + 2 + lane_id * ELEMS + i] = oi[i]; __syncthreads(); // Reduction: warp 0 combines all tiles if (warp_id == 0) { // Load first tile's results as initial accumulator float m_global = smem[0 * (HEAD_DIM + 2) + 0]; float l_global = smem[0 * (HEAD_DIM + 2) + 1]; float o_global[ELEMS]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) o_global[i] = smem[0 * (HEAD_DIM + 2) + 2 + lane_id * ELEMS + i]; // Merge remaining tiles for (int t = 1; t < TILES; t++) { float mi_t = smem[t * (HEAD_DIM + 2) + 0]; float li_t = smem[t * (HEAD_DIM + 2) + 1]; float m_new = fmaxf(m_global, mi_t); float corr_g = expf(m_global - m_new); float corr_t = expf(mi_t - m_new); l_global = l_global * corr_g + li_t * corr_t; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { float oi_t = smem[t * (HEAD_DIM + 2) + 2 + lane_id * ELEMS + i]; o_global[i] = o_global[i] * corr_g + oi_t * corr_t; } m_global = m_new; } // Handle the case where some tiles are empty (mi = -FLT_MAX) // If all tiles are empty, l_global = 0. Handle this. float inv_l = (l_global == 0.0f) ? 0.0f : 1.0f / l_global; // Store output __nv_bfloat16* o_base = output + (size_t)(b * H + h) * HEAD_DIM; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) o_base[lane_id * ELEMS + i] = __float2bfloat16(o_global[i] * inv_l); } } ``` Wait, there's an issue. The smem layout has TILES * (HEAD_DIM + 2) floats. But some tiles might be empty (my_tile_start >= total_pages). For those tiles, mi=-FLT_MAX, li=0, oi=0. The reduction handles this correctly because: - fmaxf(m_global, -FLT_MAX) = m_global - li_t * exp(-FLT_MAX - m_new) = 0 (since li_t = 0) - oi_t * corr_t = 0 (since oi_t = 0) But I also need to handle the case where the FIRST tile is empty. In that case, m_global = -FLT_MAX, l_global = 0, o_global = 0. The merge with subsequent tiles: - m_new = fmaxf(-FLT_MAX, mi_t) = mi_t - corr_g = exp(-FLT_MAX - mi_t) = 0 - l_global = 0 * 0 + li_t * exp(mi_t - mi_t) = li_t - o_global = 0 * 0 + oi_t * 1 = oi_t This is correct! The merge handles empty tiles properly. But there's a subtle issue: exp(-FLT_MAX - x) for finite x. In C++, exp(-FLT_MAX) = 0 (underflow). And -FLT_MAX - x = -FLT_MAX (since FLT_MAX >> x). So exp(-FLT_MAX) = 0. Correct. Wait, actually, -FLT_MAX - x might not be -FLT_MAX due to floating-point arithmetic. If x is finite and much smaller than FLT_MAX, then -FLT_MAX - x = -FLT_MAX (since the subtraction doesn't change the value due to the limited precision of float). And exp(-FLT_MAX) = 0. Hmm, but what if x is very negative? Like -1e10? Then -FLT_MAX - (-1e10) = -FLT_MAX + 1e10 = -FLT_MAX (still, because FLT_MAX is much larger than 1e10). OK, this is fine. Let me also handle the edge case where l_global is 0 after all merges. This happens when all tiles are empty (seq_len = 0). I already handle this with the `if (seq_len == 0)` check at the top. Actually, there's another edge case: what if total_pages > 0 but the tile has 0 valid tokens? This shouldn't happen because if total_pages > 0, each page has at least 1 valid token (the page exists because seq_len > 0). But wait, with tiling, some tiles might have 0 pages. For example, if total_pages = 10 and TILES = 4 with pages_per_tile = 3: tiles 0-2 have 3 pages each, tile 3 has 1 page. All tiles have at least 1 page. But if total_pages = 2 and TILES = 4 with pages_per_tile = 1: tiles 0-1 have 1 page each, tiles 2-3 have 0 pages. Tiles 2-3 are empty. For empty tiles, the kernel doesn't enter the page loop, so mi=-FLT_MAX, li=0, oi=0. The reduction handles this correctly. OK, the kernel should work. Let me also think about the shared memory size. Shared memory = TILES * (HEAD_DIM + 2) * sizeof(float) For TILES=4, HEAD_DIM=128: 4 * 130 * 4 = 2080 bytes For TILES=8, HEAD_DIM=128: 8 * 130 * 4 = 4160 bytes For TILES=16, HEAD_DIM=128: 16 * 130 * 4 = 8320 bytes For TILES=8, HEAD_DIM=64: 8 * 66 * 4 = 2112 bytes All are well within the shared memory limit (typically 48-96 KB per SM). Let me structure the solution as: 1. CUDA source string with kernel and launcher 2. load_inline to compile 3. Model class that calls the compiled function Here's the full code: ```python """Paged attention decode kernel using CUDA C++ with warp-level tiling.""" import math import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline OP_TYPE = "attention" SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["bf16"] HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"] BATCH = 8 NUM_HEADS = 32 NUM_KV_HEADS = 8 HEAD_DIM = 128 SEQ_LEN = 1024 PAGE_SIZE = 16 CUDA_SRC = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_bf16.h> #include <cfloat> #include <math.h> __device__ __forceinline__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int mask = 16; mask > 0; mask >>= 1) val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, val, mask); return val; } template <int HEAD_DIM, int TILES> __global__ void __launch_bounds__(TILES * 32) paged_attn_kernel( const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ query, const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ kv_cache, const int* __restrict__ block_table, const int* __restrict__ seq_lens, __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ output, const int B, const int H, const int Hkv, const int P, const int max_pages, const int group_size, const int pages_per_tile ) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; constexpr int WARP_SIZE = 32; const int warp_id = threadIdx.x / WARP_SIZE; const int lane_id = threadIdx.x % WARP_SIZE; const int bh = blockIdx.x; const int b = bh / H; const int h = bh % H; const int hkv = h / group_size; const int seq_len = seq_lens[b]; constexpr int ELEMS = HEAD_DIM / WARP_SIZE; if (seq_len == 0) { if (warp_id == 0) { __nv_bfloat16* o = output + (size_t)bh * HEAD_DIM; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) o[lane_id * ELEMS + i] = __float2bfloat16(0.0f); } return; } const float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)HEAD_DIM); // Load Q const __nv_bfloat16* q_base = query + (size_t)bh * HEAD_DIM; float q[ELEMS]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) q[i] = __bfloat162float(q_base[lane_id * ELEMS + i]); // Tile boundaries const int total_pages = (seq_len + P - 1) / P; const int tile_start = warp_id * pages_per_tile; const int tile_end = min(tile_start + pages_per_tile, total_pages); // Accumulators float mi = -FLT_MAX; float li = 0.0f; float oi[ELEMS]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) oi[i] = 0.0f; // Strides const size_t kv_bs = (size_t)P * Hkv * HEAD_DIM * 2; // block stride const size_t kv_ss = (size_t)Hkv * HEAD_DIM * 2; // slot stride const size_t kv_hs = (size_t)HEAD_DIM * 2; // head stride for (int p = tile_start; p < tile_end; p++) { const int phys = block_table[b * max_pages + p]; const __nv_bfloat16* kv_base = kv_cache + (size_t)phys * kv_bs + (size_t)hkv * kv_hs; const int ps = p * P; const int pe = min(ps + P, seq_len); for (int s = ps; s < pe; s++) { const int slot = s - ps; const __nv_bfloat16* row = kv_base + (size_t)slot * kv_ss; // QK^T float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { float ki = __bfloat162float(row[lane_id * ELEMS + i]); dot += q[i] * ki; } float score = warp_reduce_sum(dot) * scale; // Online softmax float mnew = fmaxf(mi, score); float corr = expf(mi - mnew); float es = expf(score - mnew); li = li * corr + es; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) oi[i] *= corr; // V accumulate const __nv_bfloat16* vrow = row + HEAD_DIM; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { float vi = __bfloat162float(vrow[lane_id * ELEMS + i]); oi[i] += es * vi; } mi = mnew; } } // Write partials to smem constexpr int SMEM_ROW = HEAD_DIM + 2; int off = warp_id * SMEM_ROW; if (lane_id == 0) { smem[off + 0] = mi; smem[off + 1] = li; } #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) smem[off + 2 + lane_id * ELEMS + i] = oi[i]; __syncthreads(); // Reduce: warp 0 merges all tiles if (warp_id == 0) { float mg = smem[0]; float lg = smem[1]; float og[ELEMS]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) og[i] = smem[2 + lane_id * ELEMS + i]; #pragma unroll for (int t = 1; t < TILES; t++) { float mt = smem[t * SMEM_ROW + 0]; float lt = smem[t * SMEM_ROW + 1]; float mn = fmaxf(mg, mt); float cg = expf(mg - mn); float ct = expf(mt - mn); lg = lg * cg + lt * ct; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) { float ot = smem[t * SMEM_ROW + 2 + lane_id * ELEMS + i]; og[i] = og[i] * cg + ot * ct; } mg = mn; } float inv_l = (lg == 0.0f) ? 0.0f : 1.0f / lg; __nv_bfloat16* o_base = output + (size_t)bh * HEAD_DIM; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < ELEMS; i++) o_base[lane_id * ELEMS + i] = __float2bfloat16(og[i] * inv_l); } } // Launcher dispatching on HEAD_DIM torch::Tensor launch( torch::Tensor query, torch::Tensor kv_cache, torch::Tensor block_table, torch::Tensor seq_lens, int num_kv_heads, int page_size, int group_size ) { TORCH_CHECK(query.is_cuda() && query.scalar_type() == torch::kBFloat16); const int B = query.size(0); const int H = query.size(1); const int D = query.size(2); const int P = page_size; const int max_pages = block_table.size(1); auto output = torch::empty_like(query); constexpr int TILES = 8; const int grid_size = B * H; const int block_size = TILES * 32; // Compute pages_per_tile from max seq_len in batch int max_seq = 0; for (int i = 0; i < B; i++) max_seq = max(max_seq, seq_lens[i].item<int>()); const int total_pages_max = (max_seq + P - 1) / P; const int ppt = (total_pages_max + TILES - 1) / TILES; const size_t smem = TILES * (D + 2) * sizeof(float); auto q = reinterpret_cast<const __nv_bfloat16*>(query.data_ptr<at::BFloat16>()); auto kv = reinterpret_cast<const __nv_bfloat16*>(kv_cache.data_ptr<at::BFloat16>()); auto bt = block_table.data_ptr<int32_t>(); auto sl = seq_lens.data_ptr<int32_t>(); auto out = reinterpret_cast<__nv_bfloat16*>(output.data_ptr<at::BFloat16>()); if (D == 128) { paged_attn_kernel<128, TILES><<<grid_size, block_size, smem>>>( q, kv, bt, sl, out, B, H, num_kv_heads, P, max_pages, group_size, ppt ); } else if (D == 64) { paged_attn_kernel<64, TILES><<<grid_size, block_size, smem>>>( q, kv, bt, sl, out, B, H, num_kv_heads, P, max_pages, group_size, ppt ); } else { TORCH_CHECK(false, "Unsupported head_dim: ", D); } AT_CUDA_CHECK(cudaGetLastError()); return output; } """ _mod = load_inline( name="paged_attn_cuda", cuda_sources=CUDA_SRC, functions=["launch"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "-std=c++17"], verbose=False, ) class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, batch, num_heads, num_kv_heads, head_dim, seq_len, page_size): super().__init__() assert num_heads % num_kv_heads == 0 self.batch = batch self.num_heads = num_heads self.num_kv_heads = num_kv_heads self.head_dim = head_dim self.seq_len = seq_len self.page_size = page_size self.group_size = num_heads // num_kv_heads self.scale = 1.0 / math.sqrt(head_dim) self.register_buffer("_dummy", torch.zeros(1, dtype=torch.bfloat16), persistent=False) def forward(self, query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens): return _mod.launch( query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens, self.num_kv_heads, self.page_size, self.group_size, ) def get_inputs(): B = BATCH; H = NUM_HEADS; Hkv = NUM_KV_HEADS; D = HEAD_DIM; L = SEQ_LEN; P = PAGE_SIZE pages_per_seq = (L + P - 1) // P total_pages = max(B * pages_per_seq + 8, 64) query = torch.randn(B, H, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16) * 0.1 kv_cache = torch.randn(total_pages, P, Hkv, 2 * D, dtype=torch.bfloat16) * 0.1 perm = torch.randperm(total_pages)[:B * pages_per_seq].reshape(B, pages_per_seq).int() block_table = perm.contiguous() seq_lens = torch.full((B,), L, dtype=torch.int32) return [query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens] def get_init_inputs(): return [BATCH, NUM_HEADS, NUM_KV_HEADS, HEAD_DIM, SEQ_LEN, PAGE_SIZE] ``` Wait, I have an issue with the launcher. I'm using `seq_lens[i].item<int>()` in a for loop, which accesses the tensor on the CPU. But seq_lens might be on the GPU. Let me use a different approach: ```cpp // Compute max seq_len auto sl_cpu = seq_lens.to(torch::kCPU); int max_seq = 0; for (int i = 0; i < B; i++) max_seq = std::max(max_seq, sl_cpu[i].item<int>()); ``` Or I can pass max_seq as a parameter from Python. Actually, for the benchmark, all batch elements have the same seq_len. So I can compute pages_per_tile in Python and pass it to the launcher. Let me restructure: ```python def forward(self, query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens): B, H, D = query.shape max_seq = seq_lens.max().item() pages_per_seq = (max_seq + self.page_size - 1) // self.page_size TILES = 8 pages_per_tile = (pages_per_seq + TILES - 1) // TILES return _mod.launch( query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens, self.num_kv_heads, self.page_size, self.group_size, pages_per_tile, ) ``` And in the launcher: ```cpp torch::Tensor launch( torch::Tensor query, torch::Tensor kv_cache, torch::Tensor block_table, torch::Tensor seq_lens, int num_kv_heads, int page_size, int group_size, int pages_per_tile ) { ... // No need to compute max_seq here ... } ``` This is cleaner. Let me also compute TILES in Python and pass it... but TILES is a template parameter. Let me keep it fixed at 8 for simplicity. Actually, for shape 1 (B*H=1024), TILES=8 gives 1024 * 8 * 32 = 262144 threads. Per SM: 1542. This might be too many threads, reducing occupancy. Let me use TILES=4 for shapes with B*H >= 512: ```python TILES = 8 if B * H < 512 else 4 ``` But TILES is a template parameter... I need separate kernel instantiations for different TILES values. For simplicity, let me just use TILES=8 for all shapes. The overhead for shape 1 is that some warps process fewer pages, but the total work is the same. Actually, with TILES=8 and shape 1 (128 pages per sequence), each warp processes 16 pages. With TILES=4, each warp processes 32 pages. The total per-warp work is doubled with TILES=4, but the number of warps is halved. The total work is the same. The difference is in occupancy: TILES=8 gives more warps, which helps with memory latency hiding. TILES=4 gives fewer warps but each warp does more work. For memory-bound kernels, more warps is generally better. So TILES=8 is fine even for shape 1. OK, let me finalize the code with TILES=8 and write it. Wait, actually, I just realized there might be an issue with the shared memory when TILES is large. For TILES=8 and HEAD_DIM=128: smem = 8 * 130 * 4 = 4160 bytes per block. With 1024 blocks (shape 1) and 170 SMs: 6 blocks per SM. 6 * 4160 = 24960 bytes of shared memory per SM. With 96 KB per SM: plenty of room. For register pressure: 256 threads per block * ~22 registers = 5632 registers per block. With 65536 per SM: 11 blocks max. With 6 blocks: 6 * 5632 = 33792 registers. Fine. OK, everything looks good. Let me write the final code. Oh, one more thing. I need to make sure the `reinterpret_cast` from `at::BFloat16*` to `__nv_bfloat16*` is valid. In PyTorch, `at::BFloat16` is a wrapper around `uint16_t` (the raw bf16 bits). `__nv_bfloat16` is also a wrapper around `uint16_t`. They should be layout-compatible. But to be safe, I can use `static_cast` or just access the raw pointer differently. Actually, PyTorch provides `tensor.data_ptr<at::BFloat16>()` which returns `at::BFloat16*`. The `__nv_bfloat16` type is from CUDA and has the same representation. In practice, `reinterpret_cast<const __nv_bfloat16*>(query.data_ptr<at::BFloat16>())` works and is commonly used in PyTorch CUDA extensions. OK, let me write the code. I'll also add the `AT_CUDA_CHECK` after the kernel launch for error reporting. Let me finalize the code now.

OK, let me now write the CUDA C++ kernel with tiling. The key idea is: 1. Grid: (B * H,) blocks, one per (batch, query_head) 2. Block: (TILES * 32,) threads, where each warp processes a tile of the sequence 3. After all warps finish, warp 0 reduces partial results from shared memory 4. This increases parallelism and improves memory latency hiding Let me write the full solution now.

+CUDA_SRC = r"""
+#include <torch/extension.h>
+#include <cuda.h>
+#include <cuda_bf16.h>
+#include <cfloat>
+#include <cmath>
+
+__device__ __forceinline__ float warp_reduce_sum(float v) {
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int m = 16; m > 0; m >>= 1)
+        v += __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, v, m);
+    return v;
+}
+
+// TILES warps per block, each warp handles a tile of pages.
+// After all warps finish, warp 0 reduces partial results via shared memory.
+template <int HEAD_DIM, int TILES>
+__global__ void __launch_bounds__(TILES * 32)
+paged_attn_kernel(
+    const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ query,
+    const __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ kv_cache,
+    const int* __restrict__ block_table,
+    const int* __restrict__ seq_lens,
+    __nv_bfloat16* __restrict__ output,
+    const int H, const int Hkv,
+    const int P, const int max_pages, const int group_size,
+    const int pages_per_tile
+) {
+    // Shared memory: TILES * (HEAD_DIM + 2) floats
+    // [tile][0] = mi, [tile][1] = li, [tile][2..HEAD_DIM+1] = oi
+    extern __shared__ float smem[];
+
+    constexpr int WARP = 32;
+    const int wid  = threadIdx.x / WARP;
+    const int lane = threadIdx.x % WARP;
+    const int bh   = blockIdx.x;
+    const int b    = bh / H;
+    const int h    = bh % H;
+    const int hkv  = h / group_size;
+
+    const int seq_len = seq_lens[b];
+    constexpr int E = HEAD_DIM / WARP;
+
+    if (seq_len == 0) {
+        if (wid == 0) {
+            auto o = output + (size_t)bh * HEAD_DIM;
+            #pragma unroll
+            for (int i = 0; i < E; i++)
+                o[lane * E + i] = __float2bfloat16(0.f);
+        }
+        return;
+    }
+
+    const float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)HEAD_DIM);
+
+    // --- Load Q into registers ---
+    const auto* qbase = query + (size_t)bh * HEAD_DIM;
+    float q[E];
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int i = 0; i < E; i++)
+        q[i] = __bfloat162float(qbase[lane * E + i]);
+
+    // --- Tile boundaries ---
+    const int tp = (seq_len + P - 1) / P;          // total pages
+    const int ts = wid * pages_per_tile;             // tile start page
+    const int te = min(ts + pages_per_tile, tp);     // tile end page
+
+    // --- Online-softmax accumulators ---
+    float mi = -FLT_MAX;
+    float li = 0.f;
+    float oi[E];
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) oi[i] = 0.f;
+
+    // KV-cache strides  (layout: num_blocks, P, Hkv, 2*HEAD_DIM)
+    const size_t sb = (size_t)P * Hkv * HEAD_DIM * 2;   // block stride
+    const size_t ss = (size_t)Hkv * HEAD_DIM * 2;        // slot  stride
+    const size_t sh = (size_t)HEAD_DIM * 2;               // head  stride
+
+    for (int p = ts; p < te; p++) {
+        const int phys = block_table[b * max_pages + p];
+        const auto* kb = kv_cache + (size_t)phys * sb + (size_t)hkv * sh;
+        const int ps = p * P;
+        const int pe = (ps + P < seq_len) ? ps + P : seq_len;
+
+        for (int s = ps; s < pe; s++) {
+            const auto* row = kb + (size_t)(s - ps) * ss;
+
+            // QK^T
+            float dot = 0.f;
+            #pragma unroll
+            for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) {
+                float ki = __bfloat162float(row[lane * E + i]);
+                dot += q[i] * ki;
+            }
+            float score = warp_reduce_sum(dot) * scale;
+
+            // Online softmax
+            float mn = fmaxf(mi, score);
+            float cg = expf(mi - mn);
+            float es = expf(score - mn);
+            li = li * cg + es;
+            #pragma unroll
+            for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) oi[i] *= cg;
+
+            // V accumulate
+            const auto* vr = row + HEAD_DIM;
+            #pragma unroll
+            for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) {
+                float vi = __bfloat162float(vr[lane * E + i]);
+                oi[i] += es * vi;
+            }
+            mi = mn;
+        }
+    }
+
+    // --- Write partials to shared memory ---
+    constexpr int SR = HEAD_DIM + 2;   // smem row stride
+    int soff = wid * SR;
+    if (lane == 0) { smem[soff] = mi; smem[soff + 1] = li; }
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int i = 0; i < E; i++)
+        smem[soff + 2 + lane * E + i] = oi[i];
+
+    __syncthreads();
+
+    // --- Reduce tiles (warp 0 only) ---
+    if (wid == 0) {
+        float mg
