The CUDA kernel is only marginally better than the Triton kernel (0.1528 vs 0.1460 geomean). Some shapes improved (shape 1 went from 0.329 to 0.516), but others got worse (shape 4 went from 0.058 to 0.043). The tiling doesn't seem to be helping much. Let me analyze: Shape 0: 0.145 (was 0.146) - same Shape 1: 0.516 (was 0.329) - 57% better! Shape 2: 0.090 (was 0.091) - same Shape 3: 0.287 (was 0.261) - 10% better Shape 4: 0.043 (was 0.058) - 26% worse! The issue with shape 4 (D=64) is that TILES=8 with 32 threads per warp = 256 threads per block. Each warp has ELEMS = 64/32 = 2 elements per thread. With 8 warps, the per-warp work is 125/8 ≈ 16 pages. The overhead of the tiling (shared memory, synchronization) might be hurting for this small-D case. The bigger issue is shapes 2 and 4 which are still very low. Let me think about what's really going on. For shape 2 (B=4, H=64, Hkv=8, D=128, L=4096): - 256 blocks, 256 threads per block = 65536 threads - Per SM (170 SMs): 386 threads ≈ 12 warps per SM - Unique KV data: 67 MB - Time: 0.417 ms - Achieved BW: 161 GB/s - Peak fraction: 0.090 The problem is still the redundant KV reads. With GROUP=8, the actual DRAM traffic is 8x the unique data = 537 MB. At 161 GB/s measured: 537 MB / 0.417 ms = 1288 GB/s actual throughput = 71.5% of peak. So the actual throughput is 71.5% of peak, which is decent! The low peak_fraction is because the benchmark counts unique bytes. To improve peak_fraction, I MUST reduce redundant KV reads. The GQA sharing is essential. Let me implement the GQA sharing approach. The key challenge is occupancy: with GQA sharing, we have B * Hkv programs instead of B * H. For shape 2: B * Hkv = 32 programs. With TILES=8: 32 blocks of 256 threads = 8192 threads. Per SM: 48 threads. Very low. To get more parallelism, I need to increase TILES. With TILES=64: 32 * 64 * 32 = 65536 threads. But each warp only processes 4 pages (256/64 = 4 pages). With 16 tokens per page, that's only 64 tokens per warp. The overhead might dominate. Actually, the issue is that I need both GQA sharing AND enough parallelism. The GQA sharing reduces the number of programs, but I need to compensate with more tiling. Let me think about a different approach. Instead of doing GQA sharing within a block, I can do GQA sharing across blocks using shared memory in a cooperative way. Or... I can use the two-pass approach: 1. Pass 1: Each program handles (batch, kv_head, seq_tile), loads KV once, processes all GROUP query heads 2. Pass 2: Reduce partial results per (batch, query_head) With enough tiles, this gives good occupancy. For shape 2: B * Hkv * T = 4 * 8 * T = 32T programs. For 2048 programs: T = 64. With T=64: each tile processes 256/64 = 4 pages = 64 tokens. Each program loads 2 * 64 * 128 * 2 = 32 KB of KV data. Processes 8 query heads. 32 * 64 = 2048 programs. With 4 warps each: 8192 warps. Per SM: 48 warps. OK. But each program has 8 query heads to process. With 4 warps (128 threads): 8/4 = 2 query heads per warp. Each warp processes 2 query heads sequentially for the same KV data. Actually, for the GQA approach, each program needs to process all 8 query heads. With a single warp (32 threads), processing 8 query heads means: - For each page: - Load K and V (shared) - For g = 0..7: compute QK^T, update softmax, accumulate V This is 8x more compute per page, but KV is only loaded once. With 32 threads per warp and D=128: ELEMS=4. The per-query-head state is: - q[4]: 4 registers - mi: 1 register - li: 1 register - oi[4]: 4 registers - Total: 10 registers per head For 8 heads: 80 registers per thread. Plus K, V, intermediates: maybe 100 registers total. That's feasible (max 255). But managing 8 separate softmax accumulators in registers is complex. Let me use a loop: ```cpp for (int g = 0; g < GROUP; g++) { // Load Q for head g // Process with shared K, V // Store partial results for head g } ``` The K and V are kept in registers across the g loop. For 4 KB of K and 4 KB of V: 16 * 128 = 2048 bf16 each. In fp32: 8192 registers. Way too much for registers! Wait, I'm thinking about this wrong. Each token's K is only D=128 bf16 = 64 floats (fp32) per row. For the warp-level approach: - Each thread has ELEMS=4 elements of K per token - For a page of 16 tokens: 16 * 4 = 64 K values per thread = 256 bytes - Plus V: same = 256 bytes Total per-thread for K and V of one page: 512 bytes = 128 float registers. That's way too much! I can't keep a full page of K and V in registers. I need to process token by token: 1. Load K for token s (4 values per thread) 2. Load V for token s (4 values per thread) 3. For each g: compute QK^T[g], softmax[g], accumulate V[g] 4. Move to next token But this means loading V before processing all query heads. I need to store V somewhere (registers) while processing all heads. For a single token: K[4] and V[4] = 8 registers per thread. Then for each g: - Load q_g[4]: 4 registers - Compute dot product with K: done in registers - Update mi_g, li_g: 2 registers - Update oi_g[4]: 4 registers Total per-thread registers: 8 (K,V) + 10*8 (8 query heads) = 88 registers. Plus loop vars etc: ~100. Feasible. But the q values need to be loaded from global memory for each g, which adds latency. Alternatively, load all GROUP query Q values upfront: - q[GROUP][4]: 32 registers - Plus K[4], V[4]: 8 registers - Plus mi[GROUP], li[GROUP]: 16 registers - Plus oi[GROUP][4]: 32 registers - Total: 88 registers. OK. But the online softmax for each head needs separate accumulators that persist across tokens. With GROUP=8 and D=128: oi needs 8 * 4 = 32 registers. Plus mi and li: 16 more. Total: ~120 registers. This is feasible but tight. Let me try this approach. For the kernel: - Grid: (B * Hkv * T,) - Block: (32,) threads = 1 warp per program - Each program handles (batch, kv_head, tile) - Processes all GROUP query heads for each token For shape 2 with T=64: 4 * 8 * 64 = 2048 programs. 2048 * 32 = 65536 threads. 386 per SM. Good. But there's a problem: 1 warp per block gives poor SM utilization. The SM has 32 thread slots per warp, and with 1 warp per block, the SM can only have as many warps as it has concurrent blocks. For 170 SMs with 2048 blocks: 12 blocks per SM. With 1 warp per block: 12 warps per SM = 384 threads per SM. That's OK. But the SM can support up to 64 warps (2048 threads). To get more warps, I need more blocks. With T=128: 4096 blocks = 24 per SM = 768 threads per SM. Better. Actually, let me use multiple warps per block for better utilization. Block: (4 * 32 = 128 threads). Each warp handles a different (kv_head, tile) pair. Grid: (B * Hkv * T / 4,). Hmm, this is getting complicated. Let me just use T=64 with 1 warp per block and see if it works. Actually, I realize there's a simpler approach that gives both GQA sharing and good parallelism: **process tokens in parallel across warps within a block**. Block: (GROUP * 32) threads. Two-pass tiled approach for parallelism 2. GQA sharing to reduce KV reads 3. Better memory access patterns Let me implement this in Triton. The advantage of Triton is that it handles memory coalescing and vectorization automatically. **Triton two-pass GQA approach:** Pass 1: Tiled partial attention with GQA - Grid: (B * Hkv * T,) - Each program handles (batch, kv_head, tile) - Loads K and V once, processes all GROUP query heads - Writes partial (mi[g], li[g], oi[g]) per (batch, query_head, tile) to global scratch buffers Pass 2: Reduction - Grid: (B * H,) - Each program reduces T partial results - Writes final output For the partial results: - partial_m: (B * H, T) float - partial_l: (B * H, T) float - partial_o: (B * H, T, D) float The scratch buffers are allocated in the Model.forward() method. For the Triton kernel, managing GROUP query heads in a loop: ```python @triton.jit def _tiled_gqa_kernel( q_ptr, kv_ptr, bt_ptr, sl_ptr, pm_ptr, pl_ptr, po_ptr, H: tl.constexpr, Hkv: tl.constexpr, D: tl.constexpr, P: tl.constexpr, GROUP: tl.constexpr, ..., ): pid = tl.program_id(0) bkv = pid // T # (batch * kv_head) tile = pid % T b = bkv // Hkv hkv = bkv % Hkv # Load all GROUP query Q vectors q = tl.zeros([GROUP, BLOCK_D], tl.float32) for g in range(GROUP): h = hkv * GROUP + g q[g] = tl.load(q_ptr + b * stride_qb + h * stride_qh + offs_d, ...).to(tl.float32) # Per-head accumulators mi = tl.full([GROUP], float('-inf'), tl.float32) li = tl.zeros([GROUP], tl.float32) oi = tl.zeros([GROUP, BLOCK_D], tl.float32) # Iterate over pages in this tile for pi in range(tile_start, tile_end): # Load K, V (shared) k = tl.load(k_ptrs, ...) v = tl.load(v_ptrs, ...) for g in range(GROUP): scores = tl.sum(q[g][None, :] * k, axis=1) * scale # Online softmax update for head g ... # Accumulate weighted V for head g ... # Store back to mi[g], li[g], oi[g] # Store partial results for each query head for g in range(GROUP): h = hkv * GROUP + g bh = b * H + h tl.store(pm_ptr + bh * stride_pm + tile, mi_g) tl.store(pl_ptr + bh * stride_pl + tile, li_g) tl.store(po_ptr + bh * stride_po_t + ..., oi_g) ``` The problem is managing per-head accumulators in Triton. Since GROUP is a constexpr, the loop is unrolled. The accumulators (mi, li, oi) are tensors of shape [GROUP] and [GROUP, BLOCK_D]. For the per-head update, I need to extract mi[g], update it, and write it back. In Triton, this can be done with: ```python gmask = (tl.arange(0, GROUP) == g) mi_g = tl.sum(tl.where(gmask, mi, -1e9)) # extract scalar # ... update ... mi = tl.where(gmask, mi_new, mi) # write back ``` This is the `tl.where` approach I considered earlier. Let me implement it. Actually, let me think about whether there's a cleaner way. In Triton, I can use scalar indexing when the index is a Python constant: ```python for g in range(GROUP): # GROUP is constexpr, g is a Python int at each unrolled iteration mi_g = mi[g] # scalar extraction # update mi_g mi[g] = mi_new # tensor element update ``` Wait, does Triton support `mi[g] = value` for tensor element assignment? In Triton, tensors are immutable (SSA). But with the loop unrolled, the compiler might handle this as SSA renaming. Let me test this. If it doesn't work, I'll use the `tl.where` approach. Actually, looking at Triton documentation and examples, scalar indexing on tensors IS supported for reading: `mi[g]` returns a scalar. But writing `mi[g] = value` is NOT supported because tensors are immutable. So I need to use `tl.where`: ```python gmask = (tl.arange(0, GROUP) == g) mi = tl.where(gmask, mi_new, mi) ``` This creates a new tensor at each iteration. For GROUP=4, the compiler should handle this efficiently. OK, let me implement the full two-pass GQA Triton kernel. Actually, let me take a different approach entirely. Instead of two-pass GQA, let me implement a single-pass GQA kernel using shared memory in CUDA C++. **CUDA C++ GQA kernel with shared memory:** Block: (GROUP * 32) threads (one warp per query head) Grid: (B * Hkv * T,) (tile the sequence for more parallelism) Each block: 1. All warps cooperatively load K and V for one page into shared memory 2. Each warp independently processes its query head with the shared K and V 3. __syncthreads() before loading the next page 4. After all pages in the tile, write partial results to global memory A separate reduction kernel combines partial results. For shape 2 with T=16: B * Hkv * T = 4 * 8 * 16 = 512 blocks. Each block: GROUP=8 * 32 = 256 threads. Total: 512 * 256 = 131072 threads. Per SM (170): 771 threads = 24 warps. Good! Shared memory per block: 2 * P * D * sizeof(bf16) = 2 * 16 * 128 * 2 = 8 KB. With 512 blocks and 170 SMs: 3 blocks per SM. 3 * 8 = 24 KB shared memory per SM. Fine. This should give: - GQA sharing: KV loaded once per (batch, kv_head, tile) - Good occupancy: 771 threads per SM - Shared memory: fast KV access for all warps in a block Let me implement this. For the kernel: ```cpp template <int HEAD_DIM, int GROUP, int TILES> __global__ void __launch_bounds__(GROUP * 32) paged_attn_gqa_kernel( const __nv_bfloat16* query, const __nv_bfloat16* kv_cache, const int* block_table, const int* seq_lens, float* partial_m, float* partial_l, float* partial_o, int H, int Hkv, int P, int max_pages, int group_size, int pages_per_tile, int total_tiles ) { // Shared memory: 2 * P * HEAD_DIM bf16 values // But we store in fp32 for better precision: 2 * P * HEAD_DIM floats // Actually, let's store in bf16 to save shared memory and convert when needed extern __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 smem_kv[]; __nv_bfloat16* smem_k = smem_kv; __nv_bfloat16* smem_v = smem_kv + P * HEAD_DIM; const int wid = threadIdx.x / 32; const int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; // Map program to (batch, kv_head, tile) const int pid = blockIdx.x; const int bkv_t = pid; // (batch * Hkv * tile) const int tile = bkv_t % total_tiles; const int bkv = bkv_t / total_tiles; const int b = bkv / Hkv; const int hkv = bkv % Hkv; // This warp handles query head hkv * GROUP + wid const int h = hkv * GROUP + wid; const int bh = b * H + h; const int seq_len = seq_lens[b]; constexpr int E = HEAD_DIM / 32; if (h >= H || seq_len == 0) { // Write empty partial result if (lane == 0) { partial_m[bh * total_tiles + tile] = -FLT_MAX; partial_l[bh * total_tiles + tile] = 0.f; } for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) partial_o[(bh * total_tiles + tile) * HEAD_DIM + lane * E + i] = 0.f; return; } const float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)HEAD_DIM); // Load Q const auto* qbase = query + (size_t)bh * HEAD_DIM; float q[E]; for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) q[i] = __bfloat162float(qbase[lane * E + i]); // Tile boundaries const int total_pages = (seq_len + P - 1) / P; const int tile_start = tile * pages_per_tile; const int tile_end = min(tile_start + pages_per_tile, total_pages); // Accumulators float mi = -FLT_MAX; float li = 0.f; float oi[E] = {0.f}; // Strides const size_t sb = (size_t)P * Hkv * HEAD_DIM * 2; const size_t ss = (size_t)Hkv * HEAD_DIM * 2; const size_t sh = (size_t)HEAD_DIM * 2; for (int p = tile_start; p < tile_end; p++) { const int phys = block_table[b * max_pages + p]; const auto* kv_base = kv_cache + (size_t)phys * sb + (size_t)hkv * sh; const int ps = p * P; const int pe = min(ps + P, seq_len); const int valid = pe - ps; // Cooperatively load K and V into shared memory // Total elements: 2 * P * HEAD_DIM bf16 values // Total threads: GROUP * 32 const int total_elems = valid * HEAD_DIM; // K elements const int tid = wid * 32 + lane; const int total_threads = GROUP * 32; // Load K: valid * HEAD_DIM bf16 elements for (int idx = tid; idx < total_elems; idx += total_threads) { int s = idx / HEAD_DIM; int d = idx % HEAD_DIM; smem_k[s * HEAD_DIM + d] = kv_base[(size_t)s * ss + d]; } // Load V: valid * HEAD_DIM bf16 elements for (int idx = tid; idx < total_elems; idx += total_threads) { int s = idx / HEAD_DIM; int d = idx % HEAD_DIM; smem_v[s * HEAD_DIM + d] = kv_base[(size_t)s * ss + HEAD_DIM + d]; } __syncthreads(); // Each warp processes its query head with shared K and V for (int s = 0; s < valid; s++) { // Load K from shared memory float dot = 0.f; for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) { float ki = __bfloat162float(smem_k[s * HEAD_DIM + lane * E + i]); dot += q[i] * ki; } float score = warp_reduce_sum(dot) * scale; // Online softmax float mn = fmaxf(mi, score); float cg = expf(mi - mn); float es = expf(score - mn); li = li * cg + es; for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) oi[i] *= cg; // Load V from shared memory for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) { float vi = __bfloat162float(smem_v[s * HEAD_DIM + lane * E + i]); oi[i] += es * vi; } mi = mn; } __syncthreads(); } // Write partial results to global memory if (lane == 0) { partial_m[bh * total_tiles + tile] = mi; partial_l[bh * total_tiles + tile] = li; } for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) partial_o[(bh * total_tiles + tile) * HEAD_DIM + lane * E + i] = oi[i]; } ``` And the reduction kernel: ```cpp template <int HEAD_DIM, int TILES> __global__ void __launch_bounds__(32) paged_attn_reduce_kernel( const float* partial_m, const float* partial_l, const float* partial_o, __nv_bfloat16* output, int H, int total_tiles ) { const int bh = blockIdx.x; const int lane = threadIdx.x; constexpr int E = HEAD_DIM / 32; // Load first tile float mg = partial_m[bh * total_tiles + 0]; float lg = partial_l[bh * total_tiles + 0]; float og[E]; for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) og[i] = partial_o[(bh * total_tiles + 0) * HEAD_DIM + lane * E + i]; // Merge remaining tiles for (int t = 1; t < TILES; t++) { float mt = partial_m[bh * total_tiles + t]; float lt = partial_l[bh * total_tiles + t]; float mn = fmaxf(mg, mt); float c0 = expf(mg - mn); float c1 = expf(mt - mn); lg = lg * c0 + lt * c1; for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) { float ot = partial_o[(bh * total_tiles + t) * HEAD_DIM + lane * E + i]; og[i] = og[i] * c0 + ot * c1; } mg = mn; } float inv = (lg == 0.f) ? 0.f : 1.f / lg; auto* ob = output + (size_t)bh * HEAD_DIM; for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) ob[lane * E + i] = __float2bfloat16(og[i] * inv); } ``` This is a clean two-pass approach. But I have multiple template combinations: - HEAD_DIM: 64 or 128 - GROUP: 4 or 8 - TILES: varies I can't template on TILES for all values. Let me fix TILES at a reasonable value (e.g., 16) and let some tiles be empty for shapes with fewer pages. Actually, let me use a maximum TILES value and compute pages_per_tile accordingly. If a tile has 0 pages, it produces an empty partial result, which the reduction handles correctly. Max TILES = 16 for all shapes. pages_per_tile = ceil(max_pages / 16). For shape 0: max_pages=64, ppt=4 For shape 1: max_pages=128, ppt=8 For shape 2: max_pages=256, ppt=16 For shape 3: max_pages=96, ppt=6 For shape 4: max_pages=125, ppt=8 These are reasonable page counts per tile. One more thought: the reduction kernel needs to handle the case where some tiles have empty partial results (mi=-FLT_MAX, li=0, oi=0). The merge formula handles this: - fmaxf(mg, -FLT_MAX) = mg - lt * exp(-FLT_MAX - mn) = 0 (since li_t = 0) - ot * exp(-FLT_MAX - mn) = 0 (since oi_t = 0) Wait, exp(-FLT_MAX - mn) where mn is a finite value. -FLT_MAX - mn ≈ -FLT_MAX (since FLT_MAX >> mn). exp(-FLT_MAX) ≈ 0. So lt * 0 = 0 and ot * 0 = 0. Correct. But what about the first tile being empty? If tile 0 has mi=-FLT_MAX, li=0, oi=0: - mg = -FLT_MAX, lg = 0, og = 0 - Merge with tile 1 (non-empty): mn = fmaxf(-FLT_MAX, m1) = m1 - c0 = exp(-FLT_MAX - m1) = 0 - c1 = exp(m1 - m1) = 1 - lg = 0 * 0 + l1 * 1 = l1 - og = 0 * 0 + o1 * 1 = o1 - mg = m1 This is correct! I'm going to write the full solution. Let me also think about an alternative: instead of a separate reduction kernel, I can do the reduction in Python/PyTorch. Actually, I can do the reduction with PyTorch operations: ```python # partial_m: (B*H, T) float # partial_l: (B*H, T) float # partial_o: (B*H, T, D) float # Global max m_global = partial_m.max(dim=1).values # (B*H,) correction = torch.exp(partial_m - m_global.unsqueeze(1)) # (B*H, T) l_global = (partial_l * correction).sum(dim=1) # (B*H,) o_global = (partial_o * correction.unsqueeze(2)).sum(dim=1) # (B*H, D) output = (o_global / l_global.unsqueeze(1)).to(torch.bfloat16) output = output.reshape(B, H, D) ``` This is simpler than a CUDA reduction kernel. And since the reduction data is small (B*H*T partial results), the PyTorch operations should be fast. Let me use this approach instead of a separate CUDA reduction kernel. Let me think about the access pattern. For K loading: - Thread 0 loads smem_k[0] = kv_base[0] (K for slot 0, dim 0) - Thread 1 loads smem_k[1] = kv_base[1] (K for slot 0, dim 1) - ... - Thread 127 loads smem_k[127] = kv_base[127] (K for slot 0, dim 127) - Thread 128 loads smem_k[128] = kv_base[ss] (K for slot 1, dim 0) - ... Wait, the idx to (s, d) mapping is: - idx = s * HEAD_DIM + d - s = idx / HEAD_DIM - d = idx % HEAD_DIM For consecutive threads loading consecutive idx values: - Thread 0: s=0, d=0 -> kv_base[0] - Thread 1: s=0, d=1 -> kv_base[1] - ... - Thread 127: s=0, d=127 -> kv_base[127] - Thread 128: s=1, d=0 -> kv_base[ss] For thread 0-127: consecutive addresses. Coalesced! For thread 128: address = kv_base + ss. If ss is large (Hkv * HEAD_DIM * 2 = 8 * 128 * 2 = 2048 bf16), this is a different cache line. With 256 threads and 128 elements per row: threads 0-127 load row 0, threads 128-255 load row 1. Each group of 128 threads loads a contiguous row. This is coalesced within each row. For P=16 rows and D=128: total = 2048 elements. With 256 threads, each thread loads 8 elements across the strided loop: - Iteration 0: threads 0-2047 -> but we only have 256 threads, so this wraps around - Actually, total_k_elems = valid * HEAD_DIM = 16 * 128 = 2048. With 256 threads: 2048/256 = 8 iterations. - Each thread loads 8 elements, spaced 256 apart in the linearized index. The access pattern: - Thread 0, iter 0: idx=0, s=0, d=0 - Thread 0, iter 1: idx=256, s=2, d=0 - Thread 0, iter 2: idx=512, s=4, d=0 - ... For thread 0 and thread 1 (consecutive): - Thread 0, iter 0: s=0, d=0 -> address kv_base + 0 - Thread 1, iter 0: s=0, d=1 -> address kv_base + 1 These are consecutive. Coalesced. For thread 0 and thread 128: - Thread 0, iter 0: s=0, d=0 -> address kv_base + 0 - Thread 128, iter 0: s=1, d=0 -> address kv_base + ss These are ss apart. Not coalesced in the same transaction, but each is in its own coalesced group. Overall, the access pattern should be reasonably coalesced. Each warp of 32 threads loads 32 consecutive elements within a row, which is perfectly coalesced. OK, this should work. Let me write the full code. Actually, wait. I realize I should simplify this. Let me structure the solution as follows: ```python CUDA_SRC = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_bf16.h> #include <cfloat> #include <cmath> __device__ __forceinline__ float warp_reduce_sum(float v) { #pragma unroll for (int m = 16; m > 0; m >>= 1) v += __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, v, m); return v; } // GQA tiled attention kernel // Grid: B * Hkv * T, Block: GROUP * 32 // Each warp handles one query head. const int total_threads = blockDim.x; // Map blockIdx to (batch, kv_head, tile) const int pid = blockIdx.x; const int tile = pid % num_tiles; const int bkv = pid / num_tiles; const int b = bkv / Hkv; const int hkv = bkv % Hkv; // This warp handles query head hkv * group_size + wid const int h = hkv * group_size + wid; const int bh = b * H + h; const int seq_len = seq_lens[b]; constexpr int E = HEAD_DIM / 32; // Check if this warp has a valid query head const bool valid_head = (wid < group_size) && (h < H); if (!valid_head || seq_len == 0) { if (valid_head || (h < H)) { if (lane == 0) { partial_m[bh * num_tiles + tile] = -FLT_MAX; partial_l[bh * num_tiles + tile] = 0.f; } for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) partial_o[(bh * num_tiles + tile) * HEAD_DIM + lane * E + i] = 0.f; } return; } const float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)HEAD_DIM); // Load Q const auto* qbase = query + (size_t)bh * HEAD_DIM; float q[E]; for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) q[i] = __bfloat162float(qbase[lane * E + i]); // Tile boundaries const int total_pages = (seq_len + P - 1) / P; const int tile_start = tile * pages_per_tile; const int tile_end = min(tile_start + pages_per_tile, total_pages); // Accumulators float mi = -FLT_MAX; float li = 0.f; float oi[E]; for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) oi[i] = 0.f; // KV strides const size_t sb = (size_t)P * Hkv * HEAD_DIM * 2; const size_t ss = (size_t)Hkv * HEAD_DIM * 2; const size_t sh = (size_t)HEAD_DIM * 2; for (int p = tile_start; p < tile_end; p++) { const int phys = block_table[b * max_pages + p]; const auto* kv_base = kv_cache + (size_t)phys * sb + (size_t)hkv * sh; const int ps = p * P; const int pe = min(ps + P, seq_len); const int valid = pe - ps; const int total_k_elems = valid * HEAD_DIM; // Cooperatively load K into shared memory for (int idx = tid; idx < total_k_elems; idx += total_threads) { int s = idx / HEAD_DIM; int d = idx % HEAD_DIM; smem_k[idx] = kv_base[(size_t)s * ss + d]; } // Cooperatively load V into shared memory for (int idx = tid; idx < total_k_elems; idx += total_threads) { int s = idx / HEAD_DIM; int d = idx % HEAD_DIM; smem_v[idx] = kv_base[(size_t)s * ss + HEAD_DIM + d]; } __syncthreads(); // Process each token using shared K and V for (int s = 0; s < valid; s++) { float dot = 0.f; for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) { float ki = __bfloat162float(smem_k[s * HEAD_DIM + lane * E + i]); dot += q[i] * ki; } float score = warp_reduce_sum(dot) * scale; float mn = fmaxf(mi, score); float cg = expf(mi - mn); float es = expf(score - mn); li = li * cg + es; for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) oi[i] *= cg; for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) { float vi = __bfloat162float(smem_v[s * HEAD_DIM + lane * E + i]); oi[i] += es * vi; } mi = mn; } __syncthreads(); } // Write partial results if (lane == 0) { partial_m[bh * num_tiles + tile] = mi; partial_l[bh * num_tiles + tile] = li; } for (int i = 0; i < E; i++) partial_o[(bh * num_tiles + tile) * HEAD_DIM + lane * E + i] = oi[i]; } torch::Tensor paged_attn_gqa_fwd( torch::Tensor query, torch::Tensor kv_cache, torch::Tensor bt, torch::Tensor sl, torch::Tensor pm, torch::Tensor pl, torch::Tensor po, int64_t hkv_, int64_t page_size_, int64_t group_size_, int64_t num_tiles_, int64_t ppt_ ) { const int B = query.size(0); auto pop = po.data_ptr<float>(); if (D == 128) { paged_attn_gqa_kernel<128><<<grid_size, block_size, smem>>>( qp, kvp, btp, slp, pmp, plp, pop, H, Hkv, P, MP, GS, PP, NT); } else { paged_attn_gqa_kernel<64><<<grid_size, block_size, smem>>>( qp, kvp, btp, slp, pmp, plp, pop, H, Hkv, P, MP, GS, PP, NT); For the merge: - m_global = max(mi_0, mi_1, ..., mi_T) - correction[t] = exp(mi_t - m_global) - l_global = sum(li_t * correction[t]) - o_global = sum(oi_t * correction[t]) - output = o_global / l_global Wait, but oi_t is the UNNORMALIZED output (sum of exp(score - mi_t) * V). 1	"""Paged attention decode kernel -- CUDA C++ two-pass with GQA sharing.
2	
3	Pass 1: CUDA kernel tiles the sequence across blocks. Each block handles one
4	(batch, kv_head, tile). KV is loaded cooperatively into shared memory and shared
5	across all GROUP warps (one per query head). Partial softmax stats are written
6	to global scratch buffers.
7	
8	Pass 2: PyTorch vectorized reduction merges the per-tile partials into the
9	final attention output.
10	"""
11	import math
12	
13	import torch
14	import torch.nn as nn
15	from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline
16	
17	OP_TYPE = "attention"
18	SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["bf16"]
19	HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]
20	
21	BATCH = 8
22	NUM_HEADS = 32
23	NUM_KV_HEADS = 8
24	HEAD_DIM = 128
25	SEQ_LEN = 1024
26	PAGE_SIZE = 16
27	
28	CUDA_SRC = r"""
29	#include <torch/extension.h>
30	#include <cuda.h>
31	#include <cuda_bf16.h>
32	#include <cfloat>
33	#include <cmath>
34	
35	__device__ __forceinline__ float warp_reduce_sum(float v) {
36	    #pragma unroll
37	    for (int m = 16; m > 0; m >>= 1)
38	        v += __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, v, m);
39	    return v;
40	}
41	
42	template <int HEAD_DIM>
43	__global__ void paged_attn_gqa_kernel(

This session is being continued from a previous conversation that ran out of context. The summary below covers the earlier portion of the conversation. Summary: 1. Primary Request and Intent: Write a custom paged attention decode kernel for RTX PRO 6000 (SM120 Blackwell, GDDR7, 1.8 TB/s). The kernel handles single-query decode where each batch element has a query (num_heads, head_dim) attending over a paged KV cache. Must handle 5 shapes (including non-power-of-2 seq_lens, GQA with group_size 4 and 8, head_dim 64 and 128). Correctness tolerance is 0.02 atol/rtol on bf16. Must NOT use forbidden ops (vllm, flashinfer, SDPA). The flywheel is implement → check correctness with `python check.py` → benchmark with `python benchmark.py` → iterate. "Take as long as you need to actually push the number up." 2. Key Technical Concepts: - Paged attention decode with online softmax (FlashAttention-style) - GQA (Grouped Query Attention): num_heads > num_kv_heads, group_size = num_heads/num_kv_heads - KV cache layout: (num_blocks, page_size, num_kv_heads, head_dim * 2) with [K|V] packed - Memory-bound kernel; peak bandwidth 1800 GB/s on RTX PRO 6000 Blackwell - CUDA C++ via `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` with SM120 support - Triton as an alternative kernel framework (tried first, then moved to CUDA) - Two-pass approach: tiled partial attention + reduction of partial softmax stats - Shared memory for KV sharing across GQA warps within a block - Warp-level primitives (shuffle) for dot product reduction - `__nv_bfloat16` / `at::BFloat16` interop via `reinterpret_cast` 3. Files and Code Sections: - `problems/03_paged_attention/reference.py` - Naive PyTorch paged attention (correctness oracle). Module-level shape knobs overridden by check.py/benchmark.py. Loops over batch, gathers pages, does QK^T + softmax in fp32. - `problems/03_paged_attention/shapes.py` - 5 shapes: ```python SHAPES = [ {"batch": 8, "num_heads": 32, "num_kv_heads": 8, "head_dim": 128, "seq_len": 1024, "page_size": 16}, {"batch": 32, "num_heads": 32, "num_kv_heads": 8, "head_dim": 128, "seq_len": 2048, "page_size": 16}, {"batch": 4, "num_heads": 64, "num_kv_heads": 8, "head_dim": 128, "seq_len": 4096, "page_size": 16}, {"batch": 16, "num_heads": 32, "num_kv_heads": 8, "head_dim": 128, "seq_len": 1535, "page_size": 16}, {"batch": 8, "num_heads": 16, "num_kv_heads": 4, "head_dim": 64, "seq_len": 2000, "page_size": 16}, ] ``` - `problems/03_paged_attention/problem.yaml` - regime: memory, bytes_formula uses num_kv_heads (unique KV), tolerance 0.02, forbidden ops list. - `problems/03_paged_attention/check.py` - Runs solution vs reference across all shapes, 3 seeds, checks tolerance, rejects forbidden ops by grep. - `problems/03_paged_attention/benchmark.py` - Times eager/compiled/solution, reports TFLOPS, GB/s, peak_fraction vs 1800 GB/s. Geometric mean of per-shape peak_fractions. - `problems/03_paged_attention/solution.py` - **Current solution** (CUDA C++ two-pass GQA kernel). This is the main file being iterated on. Full current content was read in the last message. Key structure: - `CUDA_SRC`: Contains `paged_attn_gqa_kernel` (templated on HEAD_DIM) with shared memory for KV, cooperative loading, per-warp query head processing. Also `gqa_fwd` launcher function. - `Model` class: Computes tiling (NT tiles), allocates scratch buffers (pm, pl, po), calls `gqa_fwd`, then does PyTorch reduction. - Forward method PyTorch reduction: ```python m_global = pm.max(dim=1).values corr = torch.exp(pm - m_global.unsqueeze(1)) l_global = (pl * corr).sum(dim=1) o_global = (po * corr.unsqueeze(2)).sum(dim=1) out = (o_global / l_global.unsqueeze(1)).to(torch.bfloat16) ``` 4. Errors and fixes: - **Triton kernel v1**: Worked correctly, achieved 0.146 geomean peak_fraction. Performance limited by low occupancy for shapes with few (B*H) pairs and redundant KV reads from GQA. - **CUDA `load_inline` missing `cpp_sources`**: Added `cpp_sources=""` parameter. - **Function name `launch` conflicts with `at::launch`**: Renamed to `paged_attn_fwd`. - **`AT_CUDA_CHECK` undefined**: Replaced with manual `cudaGetLastError()` check. - **`cudaLaunchKernel` wrong argument count**: Switched to `<<<>>>` syntax. - **C++ linker error - function not declared**: Added forward declaration in `cpp_sources` string. - **Raw string R"(...)" caused Python parsing error**: Changed to string concatenation. - **Must clean torch_extensions cache between builds**: `rm -rf ~/.cache/torch_extensions/py311_cu130/paged_attn_cuda` (or `paged_attn_gqa`). 5. Problem Solving: - Identified that GQA-induced redundant KV reads are the main bottleneck (GROUP=8 means 8x KV traffic without sharing). - L2 cache partially shares KV (~36% hit rate estimated), but not enough. - GQA sharing via shared memory gives 2.5x improvement for shape 2 (GROUP=8) and 2x for shape 4. - Current bottleneck analysis: PyTorch reduction (6+ kernel launches, ~50-70us overhead) and serial online softmax dependency chain per warp. - Occupancy varies: shapes with B*Hkv=32 (shapes 2,4) get 512 blocks with NT=16, giving decent occupancy. 6.
